<li class="nv-view"><b><a href="/wiki/Template:Ulysses_Grant_sidebar" title="Template:Ulysses Grant sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></b></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><b><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:Ulysses_Grant_sidebar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Template talk:Ulysses Grant sidebar (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></b></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><b><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Ulysses_Grant_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></b></li>

<p><b>Ulysses S. Grant</b> (born <b>Hiram Ulysses Grant</b>; April 27, 1822 – July 23, 1885) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">18th</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> (1869–77). As <a href="/wiki/Commanding_General_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Commanding General of the United States Army">Commanding General</a>, Grant worked closely with President <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> to lead the <a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union Army</a> to victory over the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederacy</a> in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. He implemented Congressional <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction</a>, often at odds with Lincoln's successor, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a>. Twice elected president, Grant led the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Republican_Party" title="History of the United States Republican Party">Republicans</a> in their effort to remove the vestiges of Confederate nationalism and slavery, protect <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African-American</a> citizenship, and support economic prosperity nationwide. His presidency has often come under criticism for tolerating corruption and in his second term leading the nation into a severe economic depression.</p>
<p>Grant graduated in 1843 from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">U.S. Military Academy</a> at <a href="/wiki/West_Point,_New_York" title="West Point, New York">West Point</a>, served in the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a> and initially retired in 1854. He struggled financially in civilian life. When the Civil War began in 1861, he rejoined the U.S. Army. In 1862, Grant took control of <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Kentucky in the American Civil War">Kentucky</a> and most of <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Tennessee in the American Civil War">Tennessee</a>, and led Union forces to victory in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shiloh" title="Battle of Shiloh">Battle of Shiloh</a>, earning a reputation as an aggressive commander. He incorporated displaced African American slaves into the Union war effort. In July 1863, after a series of coordinated battles, Grant defeated Confederate armies and seized <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg_Campaign" title="Vicksburg Campaign">Vicksburg</a>, giving the Union control of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> and dividing the Confederacy in two. After his victories in the <a href="/wiki/Chattanooga_Campaign" title="Chattanooga Campaign">Chattanooga Campaign</a>, Lincoln promoted him to lieutenant-general and Commanding General of the United States in March 1864. Grant confronted <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a> in a series of bloody battles, trapping Lee's army in their <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Petersburg" title="Siege of Petersburg">defense of Richmond</a>. Grant coordinated a series of devastating campaigns in other theaters. In April 1865, Lee surrendered to Grant at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Appomattox_Court_House" title="Battle of Appomattox Court House">Appomattox</a>, effectively ending the war. Historians have hailed Grant's military genius, and <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_and_the_American_Civil_War" title="Ulysses S. Grant and the American Civil War">his strategies</a> are featured in military history textbooks, but a minority contend that he won by brute force rather than superior strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_2004271.E2.80.93282_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_2004271.E2.80.93282-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After the Civil War, Grant led the army's supervision of Reconstruction in the former Confederate states. Elected president in 1868 and reelected in 1872, Grant stabilized the nation during the turbulent Reconstruction period, prosecuted the <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a>, and enforced civil and voting rights laws using the army and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Department of Justice</a>. He used the army to build the Republican Party in the South, based on black voters, Northern newcomers ("<a href="/wiki/Carpetbaggers" title="Carpetbaggers" class="mw-redirect">carpetbaggers</a>"), and native Southern white supporters ("<a href="/wiki/Scalawags" title="Scalawags" class="mw-redirect">scalawags</a>"). After the <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution#Participants_in_rebellion" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">disenfranchisement of some former Confederates</a>, Republicans gained majorities and African Americans were elected to Congress and high state offices. In his second term, the Republican coalitions in the South splintered and were defeated one by one as <a href="/wiki/Redeemers" title="Redeemers">redeemers</a> (conservative whites) regained control using coercion and violence. Grant's Indian peace policy initially reduced frontier violence, but is best known for the <a href="/wiki/Great_Sioux_War_of_1876" title="Great Sioux War of 1876">Great Sioux War of 1876</a>, where <a href="/wiki/George_Custer" title="George Custer" class="mw-redirect">George Custer</a> and his regiment were killed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Little_Bighorn" title="Battle of the Little Bighorn">Battle of the Little Bighorn</a>. Throughout his presidency Grant was faced with charges of corruption in executive agencies, including congressional investigations of two cabinet secretaries.</p>
<p>In foreign policy, Grant sought to increase American trade and influence, while remaining at peace with the world. His administration successfully resolved the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Claims" title="Alabama Claims"><i>Alabama</i> Claims</a> with Great Britain, ending wartime tensions. Grant avoided war with Spain over the <a href="/wiki/Virginius_Affair" title="Virginius Affair">Virginius Affair</a>, but Congress rejected his attempted <a href="/wiki/Annexation_of_Santo_Domingo" title="Annexation of Santo Domingo">annexation of the Dominican Republic</a>. In trade policy, Grant's administration implemented a <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> and sought to strengthen the dollar. His response to the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1873" title="Panic of 1873">Panic of 1873</a> gave some financial relief to <a href="/wiki/New_York" title="New York">New York</a> banking houses, but was ineffective in halting the five-year economic depression that produced high unemployment, low prices, low profits, and bankruptcies. Leaving office in 1877, he embarked on a widely praised world tour lasting over two years.</p>
<p>In 1880, Grant was unsuccessful in obtaining a Republican nomination for a third term. Facing severe investment reversals and dying of throat cancer, he completed <a href="/wiki/Personal_Memoirs_of_Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Personal Memoirs of Ulysses S. Grant">his memoirs</a>, which proved a major critical and financial success. His death in 1885 prompted an outpouring of national unity. <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">Historians' evaluations</a> were negative about his presidency before recovering somewhat beginning in the 1980s. Scholars rank his presidency below the average of other presidents. Grant's critics take a negative view of his economic mismanagement and his failed Dominican Republic annexation treaty, while admirers emphasize his concern for <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> and enforcement of civil and voting rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012b44_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012b44-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_and_career_of_Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Early life and career of Ulysses S. Grant">Early life and career of Ulysses S. Grant</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GrantBirthplace.jpg" class="image"><img alt=" White clapboard house and outbuildings behind a white fence" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/GrantBirthplace.jpg/220px-GrantBirthplace.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/GrantBirthplace.jpg/330px-GrantBirthplace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/GrantBirthplace.jpg/440px-GrantBirthplace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1728" data-file-height="1152" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GrantBirthplace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant's birthplace in <a href="/wiki/Point_Pleasant,_Ohio" title="Point Pleasant, Ohio">Point Pleasant, Ohio</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hiram Ulysses Grant was born in <a href="/wiki/Point_Pleasant,_Ohio" title="Point Pleasant, Ohio">Point Pleasant, Ohio</a>, on April 27, 1822, to Jesse Root Grant, a <a href="/wiki/Tanner_(occupation)" title="Tanner (occupation)" class="mw-redirect">tanner</a>, and Hannah (née Simpson) Grant. His ancestors Matthew and Priscilla Grant arrived aboard the <i>Mary and John</i> at <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Bay_Colony" title="Massachusetts Bay Colony">Massachusetts Bay Colony</a> in 1630.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_19813_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_19813-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant's great-grandfather fought in the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a>, and his grandfather served in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bunker_Hill" title="Battle of Bunker Hill">Bunker Hill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith21.E2.80.9322_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith21.E2.80.9322-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant's father was a <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Party</a> supporter with abolitionist sentiments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHesseltine4_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHesseltine4-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1823, the family moved to the village of <a href="/wiki/Georgetown,_Ohio" title="Georgetown, Ohio">Georgetown</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brown_County,_Ohio" title="Brown County, Ohio">Brown County, Ohio</a>, where five more siblings were born: Simpson, Clara, Orvil, Jennie, and Mary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongacre6.E2.80.937_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongacre6.E2.80.937-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Young Grant regularly attended school. While hating the tannery, he chose work on his father's farm.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxson_.26_Bach492_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxson_.26_Bach492-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Unlike his siblings, Grant was not forced to attend church by his <a href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist" class="mw-redirect">Methodist</a> parents;<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaugh10_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaugh10-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> for the rest of his life, he prayed privately and never officially joined any denomination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson_2.E2.80.933Longacre6.E2.80.937_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson_2.E2.80.933Longacre6.E2.80.937-10"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Observers, including his own son, thought he was an agnostic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaugh10_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaugh10-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> In his youth, Grant developed an unusual ability to work with and control horses. As a general he rode the strongest and most challenging horse available, and was sometimes injured in riding.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_19818.2C_10.2C_140.E2.80.9341_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_19818.2C_10.2C_140.E2.80.9341-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When Grant was 17, Congressman <a href="/wiki/Thomas_L._Hamer" title="Thomas L. Hamer">Thomas L. Hamer</a> nominated him to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">United States Military Academy</a> at <a href="/wiki/West_Point,_New_York" title="West Point, New York">West Point, New York</a>. Hamer mistakenly wrote down the name as "Ulysses S. Grant of Ohio", and this became his adopted name.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>b<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198112Smith24.2C_83_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198112Smith24.2C_83-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> His nickname became "Sam" among army colleagues at the academy since the initials "U.S." also stood for "<a href="/wiki/Uncle_Sam" title="Uncle Sam">Uncle Sam</a>". As he later recalled it, "a military life had no charms for me"; he was lax in his studies, but he achieved above-average grades in mathematics and geology.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198116Smith26.E2.80.9328_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198116Smith26.E2.80.9328-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Quiet by nature, he established a few intimate friends, including <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Tracy_Dent" title="Frederick Tracy Dent">Frederick Tracy Dent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rufus_Ingalls" title="Rufus Ingalls">Rufus Ingalls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198120Longacre18_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198120Longacre18-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant developed a reputation as a fearless and <a href="/wiki/Horse_whisperer" title="Horse whisperer" class="mw-redirect">expert horseman</a> known as a <a href="/wiki/Horse_whisperer" title="Horse whisperer" class="mw-redirect">horse whisperer</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> setting an equestrian high-jump record that stood for almost 25 years. He also studied under <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic</a> artist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Walter_Weir" title="Robert Walter Weir">Robert Walter Weir</a> and produced nine surviving artworks. He graduated in 1843, ranking 21st in a class of 39. Glad to leave the academy, his plan was to resign his commission after his four-year term of duty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198116.2C_19_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198116.2C_19-18"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite his excellent horsemanship, he was not assigned to the cavalry (assignments were determined by class rank, not aptitude), but to the <a href="/wiki/4th_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="4th Infantry Regiment (United States)">4th Infantry Regiment</a>. He was made regimental quartermaster, managing supplies and equipment, with the rank of brevet <a href="/wiki/Second_lieutenant" title="Second lieutenant">second lieutenant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith26.E2.80.9328Longacre24_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith26.E2.80.9328Longacre24-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_military_career_and_personal_life">Early military career and personal life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg" class="image"><img alt=" Engraving of young Grant in uniform" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg/220px-Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg/330px-Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg/440px-Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1122" data-file-height="1586" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Second lieutenant Ulysses S. Grant in full dress uniform in 1843</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Grant's first assignment after graduation took him to the <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Barracks" title="Jefferson Barracks" class="mw-redirect">Jefferson Barracks</a> near <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri" class="mw-redirect">St. Louis, Missouri</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith28.E2.80.9329_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith28.E2.80.9329-20"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> It was the nation's largest military base in the west, commanded by Colonel <a href="/wiki/Stephen_W._Kearny" title="Stephen W. Kearny">Stephen W. Kearny</a>. Grant was happy with his new commander, but looked forward to the end of his military service and a possible teaching career.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith30.E2.80.9333_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith30.E2.80.9333-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> He spent some of his time in Missouri visiting the family of his West Point classmate, Frederick Tracy Dent; he became engaged to Dent's sister, <a href="/wiki/Julia_Grant" title="Julia Grant">Julia</a>, in 1844.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith30.E2.80.9333_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith30.E2.80.9333-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Amid rising tensions with Mexico, Grant's unit shifted to Louisiana as part of the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_Observation" title="Army of Observation" class="mw-redirect">Army of Observation</a> under <a href="/wiki/Major_general_(United_States)" title="Major general (United States)">Major General</a> <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith35.E2.80.9337_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith35.E2.80.9337-22"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a> broke out in 1846, the Army entered Mexico. Although a quartermaster, Grant led a cavalry charge at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Resaca_de_la_Palma" title="Battle of Resaca de la Palma">Battle of Resaca de la Palma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198132.E2.80.9333_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198132.E2.80.9333-23"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Monterrey" title="Battle of Monterrey">Monterrey</a> he demonstrated his equestrian ability, by volunteering to carry a dispatch through sniper-lined streets while hanging off the side of his horse, keeping the animal between him and the enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith56.E2.80.9357_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith56.E2.80.9357-24"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a>, wary of Taylor's growing popularity, divided his forces, sending some troops (including Grant's unit) to form a new army under Major General <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198134.E2.80.9335_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198134.E2.80.9335-25"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Scott's army landed at <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">Veracruz</a> and advanced toward <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>. The army met the Mexican forces at the battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Molino_del_Rey" title="Battle of Molino del Rey">Molino del Rey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chapultepec" title="Battle of Chapultepec">Chapultepec</a> outside Mexico City. Grant was a quartermaster in charge of supplies and did not have a combat role, but he yearned for one and finally was allowed to take part in dangerous missions. At Chapultepec, men under Grant's direction dragged a disassembled <a href="/wiki/Howitzer" title="Howitzer">howitzer</a> into a church steeple, reassembled it, and bombarded nearby Mexican troops. His bravery and initiative earned him <a href="/wiki/Brevet_(military)" title="Brevet (military)">brevet</a> promotions; he became a temporary captain while his permanent rank was lieutenant. Scott's army entered the city, and the Mexicans <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo" title="Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo">agreed to peace</a> soon afterward.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson41.E2.80.9343_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson41.E2.80.9343-26"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During this war Grant studied the tactics and strategies of Scott and others, often second guessing their moves beforehand.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson458_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson458-27"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> In his <i>Memoirs</i>, he wrote that this is how he learned about military leadership, and in retrospect identified his leadership style with Taylor's. Even so, he believed that the Mexican war was wrongful and that the territorial gains from the war were designed to expand slavery. Grant reflected in 1883, "I was bitterly opposed to the measure, and to this day, regard the war, which resulted, as one of the most unjust ever waged by a stronger against a weaker nation." He opined that the Civil War was punishment inflicted on the nation for its aggression in Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198131.2C_37_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198131.2C_37-28"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grant's mandatory service expired during the war, but he chose to remain a soldier. Four years after becoming engaged, he married Julia on August 22, 1848.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198120.2C_26_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198120.2C_26-29"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_20118_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_20118-30"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> They had four children: <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Dent_Grant" title="Frederick Dent Grant">Frederick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_Jr." title="Ulysses S. Grant Jr.">Ulysses Jr.</a> ("Buck"), <a href="/wiki/Nellie_Grant" title="Nellie Grant">Ellen</a> ("Nellie"), and <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Root_Grant" title="Jesse Root Grant">Jesse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith73_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith73-31"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant's first post-war assignments took him and Julia to <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a> and then to <a href="/wiki/Sackets_Harbor,_New_York" title="Sackets Harbor, New York">Sackets Harbor, New York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198144_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198144-32"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1852, Grant's next assignment sent him west to <a href="/wiki/Fort_Vancouver" title="Fort Vancouver">Fort Vancouver</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Territory" title="Oregon Territory">Oregon Territory</a>. Julia, who was eight months pregnant with Ulysses Jr., did not accompany him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith76.E2.80.9377_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith76.E2.80.9377-33"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> While traveling overland <a href="/wiki/Maritime_history_of_California#To_California_via_Panama" title="Maritime history of California">through Panama</a>, an outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> among his fellow travelers caused 150 fatalities; Grant arranged makeshift transportation and hospital facilities to care for the sick.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198147_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198147-34"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> He debarked in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> during the height of the <a href="/wiki/California_Gold_Rush" title="California Gold Rush">California Gold Rush</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith76.E2.80.9377_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith76.E2.80.9377-33"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grant's time in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a> followed the <a href="/wiki/Cayuse_War" title="Cayuse War">Cayuse War</a>; the army was stationed there to keep peace between settlers and Indians. To supplement a military salary which was inadequate to support his family, Grant tried and failed at several business ventures, confirming Jesse Grant's belief that his son had no head for business.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198148.E2.80.9349_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198148.E2.80.9349-35"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> He was unhappy being separated from his family, and rumors circulated that he was drinking to excess.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>c<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198148.E2.80.9349_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198148.E2.80.9349-35"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Promoted to <a href="/wiki/Captain_(U.S._Army)" title="Captain (U.S. Army)" class="mw-redirect">captain</a> in the summer of 1853, Grant was assigned to command Company F, 4th Infantry, at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Humboldt_State_Historic_Park" title="Fort Humboldt State Historic Park">Fort Humboldt</a> in California. The commanding officer at Fort Humboldt, Lieutenant Colonel <a href="/wiki/Robert_C._Buchanan" title="Robert C. Buchanan">Robert C. Buchanan</a>, received reports that Grant became intoxicated off-duty while seated at the pay officer's table. In lieu of a court-martial, Buchanan gave Grant an ultimatum to resign; he did so, effective July 31, 1854, without explanation and returned to St. Louis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith86_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith86-38"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> The War Department stated on his record, "Nothing stands against his good name."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongacre55.E2.80.9358_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongacre55.E2.80.9358-39"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> After Grant's retirement, rumors persisted in the regular army of his drinking.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198155_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198155-36"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Years later, he said, "the vice of <a href="/wiki/Intemperance" title="Intemperance" class="mw-redirect">intemperance</a> (drunkenness) had not a little to do with my decision to resign."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith87.E2.80.9388Lewis328.E2.80.93332_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith87.E2.80.9388Lewis328.E2.80.93332-40"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grant was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Order_of_Odd_Fellows" title="Independent Order of Odd Fellows">Independent Order of Odd Fellows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Civilian_struggles">Civilian struggles</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hardscrabble.tiff" class="image"><img alt="small log cabin" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Hardscrabble.tiff/lossy-page1-220px-Hardscrabble.tiff.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Hardscrabble.tiff/lossy-page1-330px-Hardscrabble.tiff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Hardscrabble.tiff/lossy-page1-440px-Hardscrabble.tiff.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3511" data-file-height="2497" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hardscrabble.tiff" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Hardscrabble", the home Grant built in Missouri for his family</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At age 32, with no civilian vocation, Grant struggled through seven financially lean years. His father initially offered him a place in the <a href="/wiki/Galena,_Illinois" title="Galena, Illinois">Galena, Illinois</a>, branch of the family's tannery and leather goods business, on condition that Julia and the children stay with her parents in Missouri or with the Grants in Kentucky. Ulysses and Julia opposed another separation and declined the offer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198158.E2.80.9360_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198158.E2.80.9360-42"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1855, Grant farmed on his brother-in-law's property near <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a>, using slaves owned by Julia's father.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198158.E2.80.9360_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198158.E2.80.9360-42"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> The farm was not successful and to earn money he sold firewood on St. Louis street corners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198161_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198161-43"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> The next year, the Grants moved to land on Julia's father's farm, and built a home Grant called "<a href="/wiki/Grant%27s_Farm" title="Grant's Farm">Hardscrabble</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198158.E2.80.9360_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198158.E2.80.9360-42"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> Julia hated the rustic house, which she described as an "unattractive cabin".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198158.E2.80.9360_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198158.E2.80.9360-42"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1857, Grant acquired a slave, a thirty-five-year-old man named William Jones, from his father-in-law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith94.E2.80.9395_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith94.E2.80.9395-44"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1857" title="Panic of 1857">Panic of 1857</a> devastated farmers, including Grant, who was forced to rent out Hardscrabble the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198162_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198162-45"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Having met with no success farming, the Grants left the farm when their fourth and final child was born in 1858. Grant freed his slave in 1859 instead of selling him, at a time when slaves commanded a high price and Grant needed money badly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith94.E2.80.9395_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith94.E2.80.9395-44"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> For the next year, the family took a small house in St. Louis where he worked with Julia's cousin Harry Boggs as a bill collector, again without success.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198164_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198164-46"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1860, Jesse offered him the job in Galena without conditions, and Grant accepted. The leather shop, "Grant &amp; Perkins", sold harnesses, saddles, and other leather goods, and purchased hides from farmers in the prosperous Galena area. Grant and family moved to a rental house that year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198165.E2.80.9366_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198165.E2.80.9366-47"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grant was not politically active before the Civil War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198169.E2.80.9370_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198169.E2.80.9370-48"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> His father-in-law was a prominent Missouri <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a>, which hurt Grant's bid to become county engineer in 1859, while his father was an outspoken <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> in Galena.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a95_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a95-49"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> In the 1856 election, Grant cast his first presidential vote for the Democrat, <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a>, later saying he was really voting against <a href="/wiki/John_C._Fr%C3%A9mont" title="John C. Frémont">John C. Frémont</a>, the first Republican candidate, over concern that Frémont's anti-slavery position would motivate southern states to secede.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198169.E2.80.9370_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198169.E2.80.9370-48"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1860, he favored Democrat <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas" title="Stephen A. Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a> over <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>, and Lincoln over the Southern Democrat, <a href="/wiki/John_C._Breckinridge" title="John C. Breckinridge">John C. Breckinridge</a>. Lacking the residency requirements in Illinois at the time, he could not vote.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198169.E2.80.9370_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198169.E2.80.9370-48"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War">Civil War</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_and_the_American_Civil_War" title="Ulysses S. Grant and the American Civil War">Ulysses S. Grant and the American Civil War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_crop_of_Brady_photo.png" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of Grant in uniform leaning on a post in front of a tent" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Grant_crop_of_Brady_photo.png/220px-Grant_crop_of_Brady_photo.png" width="220" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Grant_crop_of_Brady_photo.png/330px-Grant_crop_of_Brady_photo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Grant_crop_of_Brady_photo.png/440px-Grant_crop_of_Brady_photo.png 2x" data-file-width="1828" data-file-height="1704" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_crop_of_Brady_photo.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mathew_Brady" title="Mathew Brady">Mathew Brady</a> photo of General Grant at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cold_Harbor" title="Battle of Cold Harbor">Battle of Cold Harbor</a> in 1864</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On April 12, 1861, the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> began as Confederate troops <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Sumter" title="Battle of Fort Sumter">attacked Fort Sumter</a> in Charleston, South Carolina. Two days later, President Lincoln called for 75,000 volunteers and a mass meeting was held in Galena to encourage recruitment. Recognized as a military professional, Grant was asked to lead the ensuing effort. Before the attack on Fort Sumter, Grant had not reacted strongly to Southern secession.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a121_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a121-50"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> The news of the attack came as a shock in Galena, and Grant shared his neighbors' mounting concern about the onset of war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a121_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a121-50"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> After hearing a speech by his father's attorney, <a href="/wiki/John_Aaron_Rawlins" title="John Aaron Rawlins">John Aaron Rawlins</a>, Grant found renewed energy in the Union cause.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198173.E2.80.9376.2C_80Smith107.E2.80.93108Brands_2012a121_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198173.E2.80.9376.2C_80Smith107.E2.80.93108Brands_2012a121-51"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Rawlins later became Grant's <a href="/wiki/Aide-de-camp" title="Aide-de-camp">aide-de-camp</a> and close friend during the war. Grant recalled with satisfaction that after that first recruitment meeting in Galena, "I never went into our leather store again."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198173_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198173-52"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Without any formal rank in the army, Grant helped recruit a company of volunteers and accompanied the regiment to <a href="/wiki/Springfield,_Illinois" title="Springfield, Illinois">Springfield</a>, the state capital.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a123_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a123-53"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> During this time, Grant quickly perceived that the war would be fought for the most part by volunteers and not career soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198180_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198180-54"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> Governor <a href="/wiki/Richard_Yates_(19th_century_politician)" title="Richard Yates (19th century politician)" class="mw-redirect">Richard Yates</a> offered Grant a position recruiting and training volunteer units, which he accepted, but he still wanted a field command in the regular army. He made several efforts through contacts (including Major General George B. McClellan) to acquire such a position. McClellan refused to meet him, remembering Grant's earlier reputation for drinking while stationed in California.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood43_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood43-55"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> Meanwhile, he continued serving at the training camps and made a positive impression on the volunteer Union recruits.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198181_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198181-56"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>With the aid of his advocate in Washington, Illinois congressman <a href="/wiki/Elihu_B._Washburne" title="Elihu B. Washburne">Elihu B. Washburne</a>, Grant was formally promoted to Colonel on June 14, 1861, and put in charge of disciplining the unruly <a href="/wiki/21st_Illinois_Volunteer_Infantry_Regiment" title="21st Illinois Volunteer Infantry Regiment">21st Illinois Volunteer Infantry Regiment</a>. To restore discipline, Grant had one troublemaker bound and gagged to a post for being drunk and disorderly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood45.E2.80.9346_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood45.E2.80.9346-57"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Transferred to northern Missouri, Grant was promoted by Lincoln to Brigadier General, backdated to May 17, 1861, again with Washburne's support.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith113_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith113-58"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Believing Grant was a general of "dogged persistence" and "iron will", Major General John C. Frémont assigned Grant command of troops near <a href="/wiki/Cairo,_Illinois" title="Cairo, Illinois">Cairo, Illinois</a> by the end of August 1861.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith117.E2.80.93118Catton_196329_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith117.E2.80.93118Catton_196329-59"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Under Frémont's authority Grant advanced into <a href="/wiki/Paducah,_Kentucky" title="Paducah, Kentucky">Paducah</a> and took the town without a fight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood63_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood63-60"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belmont.2C_Forts_Henry_and_Donelson">Belmont, Forts Henry and Donelson</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Belmont" title="Battle of Belmont">Battle of Belmont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Henry" title="Battle of Fort Henry">Battle of Fort Henry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Donelson" title="Battle of Fort Donelson">Battle of Fort Donelson</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png" class="image"><img alt="Union soldiers charging into battle, some injured or dying" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png/220px-Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png/330px-Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png/440px-Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="649" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Fort_Donelson.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Battle of Fort Donelson</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On November 7, 1861 Grant and his troops crossed the Mississippi to attack Confederate soldiers encamped in <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Belmont" title="Battle of Belmont">Belmont</a>, Missouri.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a151Flood71_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a151Flood71-61"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> They took the camp, but the reinforced Confederates under Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/Gideon_J._Pillow" title="Gideon J. Pillow" class="mw-redirect">Gideon J. Pillow</a> forced a retreat to Cairo. A tactical defeat, the battle nonetheless gave Grant and his volunteers confidence and experience.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198192.E2.80.9394_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198192.E2.80.9394-62"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> After Belmont, Grant asked his new commander <a href="/wiki/Henry_Halleck" title="Henry Halleck">Henry Halleck</a> (Lincoln had relieved Frémont of command) for permission to move against <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Henry" title="Battle of Fort Henry">Fort Henry</a> in Tennessee, which would open the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_River" title="Tennessee River">Tennessee River</a> to Union gunboats; Halleck agreed on condition that the attack be conducted in coordination with navy <a href="/wiki/Flag_Officer#United_States" title="Flag Officer" class="mw-redirect">Flag Officer</a> <a href="/wiki/Andrew_H._Foote" title="Andrew H. Foote" class="mw-redirect">Andrew H. Foote</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith138.E2.80.93142_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith138.E2.80.93142-63"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant's troops, in close cooperation with Foote's naval forces captured Fort Henry on February 6, 1862.</p>
<p>Emboldened by Lincoln's call for a general advance of all Union forces, Grant ordered an immediate assault on nearby Fort Donelson, which dominated the <a href="/wiki/Cumberland_River" title="Cumberland River">Cumberland River</a> (this time without Halleck's permission). On February 15, Grant and Foote met stiff resistance from Confederate forces under Pillow. Reinforced by 10,000 troops, Grant's army totaled 25,000 troops against 12,000 Confederates. Foote's first approach was repulsed, and the Confederates attempted a breakout, pushing Grant's right flank into disorganized retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith141.E2.80.93164Brands_2012a164.E2.80.93165_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith141.E2.80.93164Brands_2012a164.E2.80.93165-64"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant rallied his troops, resumed the offensive, retook the Union right, and attacked Pillow's left. Pillow ordered Confederate troops back into the fort and relinquished command to Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/Simon_Bolivar_Buckner" title="Simon Bolivar Buckner">Simon Bolivar Buckner</a>, who the next day acceded to Grant's demand for his "unconditional and immediate surrender." Lincoln promoted Grant to major-general of volunteers while the Northern press treated Grant as a hero. Playing off his initials, they took to calling him "Unconditional Surrender Grant".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a164.E2.80.93165Smith125.E2.80.93134_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a164.E2.80.93165Smith125.E2.80.93134-65"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shiloh">Shiloh</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Union artillery firing through the woods; a messenger runs behind the line" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg/220px-Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg/330px-Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg/440px-Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="9702" data-file-height="7018" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thure_de_Thulstrup_-_Battle_of_Shiloh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Battle of Shiloh</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shiloh" title="Battle of Shiloh">Battle of Shiloh</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_Order_No._11_(1862)" title="General Order No. 11 (1862)">General Order No. 11 (1862)</a></div>
<p>Encamped on the western bank of the Tennessee River, Grant's <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Tennessee" title="Army of the Tennessee">Army of the Tennessee</a>, now numbering about 45,000 troops, prepared to attack a Confederate army of roughly equal strength at <a href="/wiki/Corinth,_Mississippi" title="Corinth, Mississippi">Corinth, Mississippi</a>, a vital railroad junction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201253.2C_63_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201253.2C_63-66"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> The Confederates, led by Generals <a href="/wiki/Albert_Sidney_Johnston" title="Albert Sidney Johnston">Albert Sidney Johnston</a> and <a href="/wiki/P.G.T._Beauregard" title="P.G.T. Beauregard" class="mw-redirect">P.G.T. Beauregard</a>, struck first on April 6, 1862, attacking five divisions of Grant's army bivouacked at Pittsburg Landing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981111_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981111-67"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant's troops were not entrenched and were taken by surprise, falling back before the Confederate onslaught.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201251.2C_94_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201251.2C_94-68"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> At day's end, the Confederates captured one Union division, but Grant's army was able to hold the Landing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201258.E2.80.9359_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201258.E2.80.9359-69"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> The remaining Union army might have been destroyed, but the Confederates halted due to exhaustion, confusion, and a lack of reinforcements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981114Flood109.2C_112Bonekemper_201259_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981114Flood109.2C_112Bonekemper_201259-70"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant, bolstered by 18,000 fresh troops from the divisions of Major Generals <a href="/wiki/Don_Carlos_Buell" title="Don Carlos Buell">Don Carlos Buell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lew_Wallace" title="Lew Wallace">Lew Wallace</a>, counterattacked at dawn the next day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201263_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201263-71"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> The Northerners regained the field and forced the rebels to retreat back to Corinth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201263.E2.80.9364_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201263.E2.80.9364-72"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After the battle the Northern press criticized Grant for high casualties and for his alleged drunkenness during the battle; as a result, Halleck removed Grant from active command of his army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a188.E2.80.93189_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a188.E2.80.93189-73"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Discouraged and disappointed, Grant considered resigning his commission, but Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/William_Tecumseh_Sherman" title="William Tecumseh Sherman">William Tecumseh Sherman</a>, one of his division commanders, convinced him to stay. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a187.2C_191_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a187.2C_191-74"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> The battle was the costliest in American history to that point, with total casualties of about 23,800.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201294_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201294-75"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln overruled Grant's critics, saying "I can't spare this man; he fights," and reinstated Grant as field commander of the Army of the Tennessee after his troops took Corinth. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongacre137_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongacre137-76"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> On September 19, 1862, Grant's army defeated Confederates at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Iuka" title="Battle of Iuka">Battle of Iuka</a>, then successfully defended <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Corinth" title="Second Battle of Corinth">Corinth</a>, inflicting heavy casualties on the enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a211.E2.80.93212_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a211.E2.80.93212-77"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> In November 1862, after Lincoln's preliminary <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a>, Grant ordered units under his command, headed by Chaplain <a href="/wiki/John_Eaton_(general)" title="John Eaton (general)">John Eaton</a>, to incorporate contraband <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> slaves into the Union war effort, giving them clothes, shelter, and wages for their services.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood133_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood133-78"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Along with his military responsibilities Grant was concerned over an expanding illicit cotton trade in his district.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith225Flood143_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith225Flood143-79"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> He believed the trade undermined the Union war effort, funded the Confederacy, and prolonged the war, while Union soldiers died in the fields.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood143.E2.80.93144Sarna_2012a37_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood143.E2.80.93144Sarna_2012a37-80"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> On December 17, 1862, he issued <a href="/wiki/General_Order_No._11_(1862)" title="General Order No. 11 (1862)">General Order No. 11</a>, expelling "Jews, as a class," from the district, saying that Jewish merchants were violating trade regulations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a218_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a218-81"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Writing in 2012, historian <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_D._Sarna" title="Jonathan D. Sarna" class="mw-redirect">Jonathan D. Sarna</a> said Grant "issued the most notorious anti-Jewish official order in American history."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESarna_2012bsee_also.2CSmith225_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESarna_2012bsee_also.2CSmith225-82"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Historians' opinions vary on Grant's motives for issuing the order.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith226.E2.80.93227Simpson164.E2.80.93165Brands_2012a217.E2.80.93219_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith226.E2.80.93227Simpson164.E2.80.93165Brands_2012a217.E2.80.93219-83"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Jewish leaders complained to Lincoln while the Northern press criticized Grant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012aSmith226.E2.80.93227_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012aSmith226.E2.80.93227-84"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln demanded the order be revoked and Grant rescinded it within three weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith226.E2.80.93227_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith226.E2.80.93227-85"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant made amends with the Jewish community during his presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESarna_2012a89.2C_147_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESarna_2012a89.2C_147-86"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vicksburg">Vicksburg</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg_Campaign" title="Vicksburg Campaign">Vicksburg Campaign</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:388px;max-width:388px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:VicksburgBlockade.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A line of about a dozen Union gunboats on the Mississippi River exchange fire with the town above on a cliff" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/VicksburgBlockade.jpg/180px-VicksburgBlockade.jpg" width="180" height="128" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/VicksburgBlockade.jpg/270px-VicksburgBlockade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/VicksburgBlockade.jpg/360px-VicksburgBlockade.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1300" data-file-height="924" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Grant's gamble: Porter's gunboats running the Confederate gauntlet at Vicksburg</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Jackson_(cropped).tif" class="image"><img alt="Lines of soldiers fire at each other, with houses in the background" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Battle_of_Jackson_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-200px-Battle_of_Jackson_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg" width="200" height="126" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Battle_of_Jackson_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-300px-Battle_of_Jackson_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Battle_of_Jackson_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-400px-Battle_of_Jackson_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7070" data-file-height="4466" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jackson,_Mississippi" title="Battle of Jackson, Mississippi">Battle of Jackson</a>, fought on May 14, 1863, was part of the <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg_Campaign" title="Vicksburg Campaign">Vicksburg Campaign</a>.</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Located on the high bluffs of the Mississippi River, <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg,_Mississippi" title="Vicksburg, Mississippi">Vicksburg, Mississippi</a> was the last obstacle to Union control of that river; both Lincoln and Grant saw it as the key to victory in the West and were determined to take the rebel stronghold. Grant was aggravated to learn that Lincoln authorized Major General <a href="/wiki/John_A._McClernand" title="John A. McClernand" class="mw-redirect">John A. McClernand</a> to raise a separate army for the purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a221.E2.80.93223_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a221.E2.80.93223-87"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> Halleck ordered McClernand to <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis</a>, and placed him under Grant's authority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood147.E2.80.93148_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood147.E2.80.93148-88"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> In December 1862, Grant moved to take Vicksburg by an overland route, with a joint water expedition on the Mississippi led by Sherman. Confederate cavalry raiders stalled Grant's advance by capturing his supply depot at Holly Springs, while the Confederate army led by Lieutenant General <a href="/wiki/John_C._Pemberton" title="John C. Pemberton">John C. Pemberton</a> concentrated and repulsed Sherman's direct approach to Vicksburg at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chickasaw_Bayou" title="Battle of Chickasaw Bayou">Chickasaw Bayou</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a223.2C_225Bonekemper_2012147-148_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a223.2C_225Bonekemper_2012147-148-89"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> McClernand reached Sherman's army, assumed command, and independently of Grant led a campaign that captured Confederate <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Arkansas_Post" title="Battle of Arkansas Post">Fort Hindman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_2012148_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_2012148-90"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> On January 29, 1863 Grant assumed personal overall command and then began a series of attempts to move his army through the water-logged terrain to bypass Vicksburg's guns; these also proved ineffective, however, Union soldiers became better trained.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_2012148-149_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_2012148-149-91"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On April 16, 1863, Grant ordered Admiral <a href="/wiki/David_Dixon_Porter" title="David Dixon Porter">David Porter</a>'s Union gunboats south under fire from the Vicksburg batteries to meet up with his Union troops who had marched south down the west side of the Mississippi River.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a226.E2.80.93228_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a226.E2.80.93228-92"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant ordered diversionary battles, confusing Pemberton and allowing Grant's army to cross east over the Mississippi, landing troops at <a href="/wiki/Bruinsburg,_Mississippi" title="Bruinsburg, Mississippi">Bruinsburg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood160_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood160-93"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> Continuing east, he captured <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jackson,_Mississippi" title="Battle of Jackson, Mississippi">Jackson</a>, the state capital and a railroad supply center. At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Champion_Hill" title="Battle of Champion Hill">Battle of Champion Hill</a>, Pemberton's army was defeated, and forced to retreat into Vicksburg.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood164.E2.80.93165_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood164.E2.80.93165-94"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> After Grant's men assaulted the Vicksburg entrenchments twice, suffering severe losses, they settled in for <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Vicksburg" title="Siege of Vicksburg">a siege lasting seven weeks</a>. Pemberton surrendered Vicksburg to Grant on July 4, 1863.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981122.E2.80.93138Smith206.E2.80.93257_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981122.E2.80.93138Smith206.E2.80.93257-95"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The fall of Vicksburg gave Union forces control over the Mississippi River and split the Confederacy in two. By that time, Grant's political sympathies fully coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Radical_Republicans" title="Radical Republicans" class="mw-redirect">Radical Republicans</a>' aggressive prosecution of the war and emancipation of the slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton_19688_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton_19688-96"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> Although the success at Vicksburg was a great morale boost for the Union war effort, Grant received criticism for his decisions and his alleged drunkenness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981128.2C_135_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981128.2C_135-97"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> The personal rivalry between McClernand and Grant continued after Vicksburg, until Grant removed McClernand from command when he contravened Grant by publishing an order without permission.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981136_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981136-98"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> When Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/Edwin_M._Stanton" title="Edwin M. Stanton" class="mw-redirect">Edwin M. Stanton</a> suggested Grant be brought back east to run the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Potomac" title="Army of the Potomac">Army of the Potomac</a>, Grant demurred, writing that he knew the geography and resources of the West better and he did not want to upset the chain of command in the East.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton_19687_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton_19687-99"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chattanooga_and_promotion">Chattanooga and promotion</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chattanooga_Campaign" title="Chattanooga Campaign">Chattanooga Campaign</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Mission_Ridge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Confederate soldiers face Union troops running toward them" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Battle_of_Mission_Ridge.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Mission_Ridge.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Battle_of_Mission_Ridge.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Mission_Ridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Battle_of_Mission_Ridge.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Mission_Ridge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="443" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Mission_Ridge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Union troops swarm <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Missionary_Ridge" title="Battle of Missionary Ridge">Missionary Ridge</a> and defeat Bragg's army.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Lincoln commissioned Grant a major general in the regular army and assigned him command of the newly formed <a href="/wiki/Military_Division_of_the_Mississippi" title="Military Division of the Mississippi">Division of the Mississippi</a> in October 1863, including the Armies of the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Ohio" title="Army of the Ohio">Ohio</a>, Tennessee, and <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Cumberland" title="Army of the Cumberland">Cumberland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewell_.26_Shrader28Brands_2012a_265_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewell_.26_Shrader28Brands_2012a_265-100"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chickamauga" title="Battle of Chickamauga">Battle of Chickamauga</a>, the Army of the Cumberland retreated into Chattanooga, where they were trapped.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood196_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood196-101"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> When informed of the situation, Grant put Major General <a href="/wiki/George_H._Thomas" title="George H. Thomas" class="mw-redirect">George H. Thomas</a> in charge of the besieged army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood201_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood201-102"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> Taking command, Grant arrived in Chattanooga by horseback, devising plans to resupply the city and break the siege.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a267McFeely_1981145_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a267McFeely_1981145-103"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln also sent Major General <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Hooker" title="Joseph Hooker">Joseph Hooker</a> and two divisions of the Army of the Potomac to assist.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a267.E2.80.93268_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a267.E2.80.93268-104"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> Union forces captured Brown's Ferry and opened a supply line to Bridgeport.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981147Smith267.E2.80.93268Catton_196846.2C_54_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981147Smith267.E2.80.93268Catton_196846.2C_54-105"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> On November 23, 1863, Grant organized three armies to attack at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Missionary_Ridge" title="Battle of Missionary Ridge">Missionary Ridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lookout_Mountain" title="Battle of Lookout Mountain">Lookout Mountain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood214.E2.80.93215_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood214.E2.80.93215-106"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> Two days later in the early morning, Hooker's forces successfully took Lookout Mountain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood214.E2.80.93215_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood214.E2.80.93215-106"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant ordered Thomas and the Army of the Cumberland to advance when Sherman's army failed to take Missionary Ridge from the northeast.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood216_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood216-107"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> The Army of the Cumberland, led by Major General <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sheridan" title="Philip Sheridan">Philip Sheridan</a> and Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/Thomas_J._Wood" title="Thomas J. Wood">Thomas J. Wood</a>, charged uphill and captured the Confederate entrenchments on top of the ridge, forcing the rebels into disorganized retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood217.E2.80.93218_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood217.E2.80.93218-108"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> The decisive battle gave the Union control of Tennessee and opened <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, the heartland of the Confederacy, to Union invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981148.E2.80.93150_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981148.E2.80.93150-109"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On March 3, 1864 Lincoln promoted Grant to <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_general_(United_States)" title="Lieutenant general (United States)">Lieutenant General</a>, giving him command of all Union Armies, under direct supervision only to the President.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood232McFeely_1981148_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood232McFeely_1981148-110"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant assigned Sherman the Division of the Mississippi and traveled east to Washington D.C., meeting with Lincoln to devise a strategy of <a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">total war</a> against the Confederacy. After settling Julia into a house in Georgetown, Grant established his headquarters with General <a href="/wiki/George_Meade" title="George Meade">George Meade</a>'s Army of the Potomac in Culpeper, Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981156_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981156-111"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> He devised a strategy of coordinated Union offensives, attacking the rebel armies at the same time to keep the Confederates from shifting reinforcements within their interior lines. Sherman was to pursue <a href="/wiki/Joseph_E._Johnston" title="Joseph E. Johnston">Joseph E. Johnston</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Tennessee" title="Army of the Tennessee">Army of the Tennessee</a>, while Meade would lead the Army of the Potomac, with Grant in camp, to attack <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Army_of_Northern_Virginia" title="Army of Northern Virginia">Army of Northern Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton_1960190.2C_193_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton_1960190.2C_193-112"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> Major General <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Butler_(politician)" title="Benjamin Butler (politician)">Benjamin Butler</a> was to advance towards Richmond from the south, up the <a href="/wiki/James_River" title="James River">James River</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981157_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981157-113"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> If Lee was forced south as expected, Grant would join forces with Butler's armies and be fed supplies from the James. Major General <a href="/wiki/Franz_Sigel" title="Franz Sigel">Franz Sigel</a> was to capture the railroad line at <a href="/wiki/Lynchburg,_Virginia" title="Lynchburg, Virginia">Lynchburg</a>, move east, and attack from the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Ridge_Mountains" title="Blue Ridge Mountains">Blue Ridge Mountains</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981157.E2.80.93175Smith313.E2.80.93339.2C_343.E2.80.93368_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981157.E2.80.93175Smith313.E2.80.93339.2C_343.E2.80.93368-114"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant knew that Lee had limited manpower and that a war of attrition fought on a battlefield without entrenchments would lead to Lee's defeat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981162.E2.80.93163_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981162.E2.80.93163-115"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grant was now riding a rising tide of popularity, and there was talk that a Union victory early in the year could lead to his candidacy for the presidency. Grant was aware of the rumors, but had ruled out a political candidacy; the possibility would soon vanish with delays on the battlefield.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981162.E2.80.93163_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981162.E2.80.93163-115"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overland_Campaign_and_Union_victory">Overland Campaign and Union victory</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Overland_Campaign" title="Overland Campaign">Overland Campaign</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:403px;max-width:403px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:197px;max-width:197px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_the_Wilderness.png" class="image"><img alt="depiction of soldiers in confused fight amid forest" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Battle_of_the_Wilderness.png/195px-Battle_of_the_Wilderness.png" width="195" height="141" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Battle_of_the_Wilderness.png/293px-Battle_of_the_Wilderness.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Battle_of_the_Wilderness.png/390px-Battle_of_the_Wilderness.png 2x" data-file-width="1724" data-file-height="1246" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The Battle of the Wilderness began almost a year of bloody fighting between forces commanded by Grant and Lee</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Lee_Surrenders_to_Grant_at_Appomattox.jpg" class="image"><img alt="drawing of men in uniform sitting and standing in parlor" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Lee_Surrenders_to_Grant_at_Appomattox.jpg/200px-Lee_Surrenders_to_Grant_at_Appomattox.jpg" width="200" height="142" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Lee_Surrenders_to_Grant_at_Appomattox.jpg/300px-Lee_Surrenders_to_Grant_at_Appomattox.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Lee_Surrenders_to_Grant_at_Appomattox.jpg/400px-Lee_Surrenders_to_Grant_at_Appomattox.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8707" data-file-height="6197" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Lee surrendering to Grant at <a href="/wiki/McLean_House_(Appomattox,_Virginia)" title="McLean House (Appomattox, Virginia)">McLean House</a>. Those depicted also include, Custer, Babcock, Sheridan, Rawlins, Meade, and Parker.</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sigel's and Butler's efforts sputtered, and Grant was left alone to fight Lee in a series of bloody battles known as the <a href="/wiki/Overland_Campaign" title="Overland Campaign">Overland Campaign</a>. Grant crossed the <a href="/wiki/Rapidan_River" title="Rapidan River">Rapidan River</a> on May 4, 1864, and attacked Lee in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Wilderness" title="Battle of the Wilderness">Battle of the Wilderness</a>, a hard-fought three-day battle with many casualties. Rather than retreat as his predecessors had done, Grant flanked Lee's army to the southeast and attempted to wedge his forces between Lee and Richmond at <a href="/wiki/Spotsylvania_Court_House,_Virginia" title="Spotsylvania Court House, Virginia" class="mw-redirect">Spotsylvania Court House</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981165_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981165-116"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Lee's army got to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Spotsylvania_Court_House" title="Battle of Spotsylvania Court House">Spotsylvania</a> first, and a costly battle ensued, lasting thirteen days. During the battle, Grant attempted to break through Lee's line of defense, resulting in one of the bloodiest assaults of the Civil War, known as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Bloody_Angle" title="Battle of the Bloody Angle" class="mw-redirect">Battle of the Bloody Angle</a>. Unable to break Lee's defenses, Grant again flanked the Confederate army to the southeast, meeting at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_North_Anna" title="Battle of North Anna">North Anna</a>, where a battle lasted three days.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981169_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981169-117"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> The Confederates had the defensive advantage, and Grant maneuvered his army to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cold_Harbor" title="Battle of Cold Harbor">Cold Harbor</a>, a vital railroad hub that linked to Richmond, but Lee's men were again able to entrench against the Union assault. During the third day of the thirteen-day battle, Grant led a costly assault on Lee's trenches. As casualty reports became known in the North, heavy criticism fell on Grant, who was castigated as "the Butcher" by the Northern press after taking 52,788 casualties in the thirty days since crossing the Rapidan. Lee's army suffered 32,907 casualties, but he was less able to replace them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201141.E2.80.9342_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonekemper_201141.E2.80.9342-118"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> The costly Union assault at Cold Harbor was the second of two battles in the war that Grant later said he regretted (the other being his initial assault on the fortifications around Vicksburg). Without being detected by Lee, Grant pulled out of Cold Harbor and moved his army south of the James River, freed Butler from <a href="/wiki/Bermuda_Hundred_Campaign" title="Bermuda Hundred Campaign">the Bermuda Hundred</a> (where the rebels had surrounded his army), and advanced toward Petersburg, Richmond's central railroad hub.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981157.E2.80.93175Smith313.E2.80.9339.2C_343.E2.80.9368_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981157.E2.80.93175Smith313.E2.80.9339.2C_343.E2.80.9368-119"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After crossing the James River undetected, Grant and the Army of the Potomac <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Petersburg" title="Battle of Petersburg" class="mw-redirect">arrived at Petersburg</a>. Confederate General P.G.T. Beauregard defended the city, and Lee's veteran reinforcements soon arrived. The result was a nine-month-long siege of Petersburg, stalling the advance. Northern resentment grew as the war dragged on, but an indirect benefit of the Petersburg siege was that Lee was forced to entrench and defend Richmond, and was unable to reinforce the Army of the Tennessee. Sheridan was assigned command of the <a href="/wiki/Union_Army_of_the_Shenandoah" title="Union Army of the Shenandoah">Union Army of the Shenandoah</a> and Grant directed him to "follow the enemy to their death".
