Lee had sent General <a href="/wiki/Jubal_Early" title="Jubal Early">Jubal Early</a> up the Shenandoah Valley to attack the federal capital and draw troops away from the Army of the Potomac, but Sheridan defeated Early, ensuring that Washington would not be endangered. Grant then ordered Sheridan's cavalry to destroy vital Confederate supplies in the Shenandoah Valley. When Sheridan reported suffering attacks by irregular Confederate cavalry under <a href="/wiki/John_S._Mosby" title="John S. Mosby">John S. Mosby</a>, Grant recommended rounding up their families for imprisonment as hostages at Fort McHenry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981178.E2.80.93186_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981178.E2.80.93186-120"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grant approved of a plan <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Crater" title="Battle of the Crater">to blow up part of the enemy trenches</a> from an underground tunnel. The explosion created a crater, into which poorly-led Union troops poured. Recovering from the surprise, Confederates surrounded the crater and easily picked off Union troops within it. The Union's 3500 casualties outnumbered the Confederates' by three-to-one; although the plan could have been successful if implemented correctly, Grant admitted the tactic had been a "stupendous failure".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981179Smith369.E2.80.93395Catton_1968308.E2.80.93309_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981179Smith369.E2.80.93395Catton_1968308.E2.80.93309-121"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> On August 9, 1864, Grant, who had just arrived at his headquarters in <a href="/wiki/City_Point,_Virginia" title="City Point, Virginia">City Point</a>, narrowly escaped death when Confederate spies blew up an ammunition barge in the James River.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton_1968349_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton_1968349-122"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> Rather than fight Lee in a full frontal attack as he had done at Cold Harbor, Grant continued to extend Lee's defenses south and west of Petersburg, to capture vital railroad links.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton_1968309_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton_1968309-123"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> As Grant continued to push the Union advance westward, Lee's lines became overstretched and undermanned. After the Federal army rebuilt the <a href="/wiki/City_Point_Railroad" title="City Point Railroad">City Point Railroad</a>, Grant was able to use mortars to attack Lee's entrenchments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton_1968294_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton_1968294-124"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> On September 2, Sherman captured <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Atlanta" title="Battle of Atlanta">Atlanta</a> while confederate forces retreated, ensuring Lincoln's reelection in November.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton_1960223.2C_228_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton_1960223.2C_228-125"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Sherman convinced Grant and Lincoln to send a Union Army to march to <a href="/wiki/Sherman%27s_March_to_the_Sea" title="Sherman's March to the Sea">Savannah</a> devastating the Confederate heartland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton_1960235_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton_1960235-126"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Once Sherman reached the East Coast and Thomas dispatched <a href="/wiki/John_Bell_Hood" title="John Bell Hood">John Bell Hood</a>'s forces in Tennessee, Union victory appeared certain, and Lincoln attempted negotiations. He enlisted <a href="/wiki/Francis_Preston_Blair" title="Francis Preston Blair">Francis Preston Blair</a> to carry a message to Confederate President Jefferson Davis. Davis and Lincoln each appointed commissioners, but the conference soon stalled. Grant contacted Lincoln, who agreed to personally meet with the commissioners at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Monroe" title="Fort Monroe">Fort Monroe</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Hampton_Roads_Conference" title="Hampton Roads Conference">peace conference</a> that took place near Union-controlled Fort Monroe was ultimately fruitless, but represented Grant's first foray into diplomacy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981198.E2.80.93210_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981198.E2.80.93210-127"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting of four men conferring in a ship's cabin, entitled &quot;The Peacemakers&quot;." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg/220px-The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg/330px-The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg/440px-The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2254" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Peacemakers_1868.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant (center left) next to Lincoln with General <a href="/wiki/William_Tecumseh_Sherman" title="William Tecumseh Sherman">Sherman</a> (far left) and Admiral <a href="/wiki/David_Dixon_Porter" title="David Dixon Porter">Porter</a> (right) — <i><a href="/wiki/The_Peacemakers" title="The Peacemakers">The Peacemakers</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In late March 1865, Grant's forces finally took Petersburg, then captured Richmond that April. Grant, Sherman, Admiral Porter and Lincoln held a conference on the <i>River Queen</i> to discuss <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction</a> of the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981213.E2.80.93214_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981213.E2.80.93214-128"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> Lee's troops began deserting in large numbers; disease and lack of supplies also diminished the remaining Confederates. Lee attempted to link up with the remnants of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_E._Johnston" title="Joseph E. Johnston">Joseph E. Johnston</a>'s defeated army, but Union cavalry forces led by Sheridan were able to stop the two armies from converging. Lee and his army surrendered to Grant at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Appomattox_Court_House" title="Battle of Appomattox Court House">Appomattox Court House</a> on April 9, 1865. Grant gave generous terms; Confederate troops surrendered their weapons and were allowed to return to their homes with their mounts, on the condition that they would not take up arms against the United States. On April 26, Johnson's Confederate army surrendered to Sherman under the same terms Grant offered to Lee. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatton_1960304_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatton_1960304-129"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> On May 26, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Kirby_Smith" title="Edmund Kirby Smith">Kirby Smith's</a> western Confederate army surrendered and the Civil War was over. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981212.2C_219.E2.80.93220Catton_1960304_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981212.2C_219.E2.80.93220Catton_1960304-130"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lincoln.27s_assassination">Lincoln's assassination</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Assassination of Abraham Lincoln">Assassination of Abraham Lincoln</a></div>
<p>On April 14, five days after Grant's victory at Appomattox, he attended a cabinet meeting in Washington. Lincoln invited him and his wife to <a href="/wiki/Ford%27s_Theater" title="Ford's Theater" class="mw-redirect">Ford's Theater</a>, but they declined as they had plans to travel to Philadelphia. In a conspiracy that targeted several government leaders, Lincoln was fatally shot by <a href="/wiki/John_Wilkes_Booth" title="John Wilkes Booth">John Wilkes Booth</a> at the theater, and died the next morning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981224_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981224-131"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> Many, including Grant himself, thought that he had been a target in the plot.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a375.E2.80.93376_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a375.E2.80.93376-132"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> Secretary of War Stanton notified him of the President's death and summoned him back to Washington. Attending Lincoln's funeral on April 19, Grant stood alone and wept openly; he later said Lincoln was "the greatest man I have ever known."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith409.E2.80.93412_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith409.E2.80.93412-133"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> Regarding the new President, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a>, Grant told Julia that he dreaded the change in administrations; he judged Johnson's attitude toward white southerners as one that would "make them unwilling citizens", and initially thought that with President Johnson, "Reconstruction has been set back no telling how far."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981227.E2.80.93229_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981227.E2.80.93229-134"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commanding_general">Commanding general</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_as_commanding_general,_1865%E2%80%931869" title="Ulysses S. Grant as commanding general, 1865–1869">Ulysses S. Grant as commanding general, 1865–1869</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transition_to_peacetime">Transition to peacetime</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USGrantHomeGalena.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Two-story red brick house where Grant lived in Galena." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/USGrantHomeGalena.jpg/220px-USGrantHomeGalena.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/USGrantHomeGalena.jpg/330px-USGrantHomeGalena.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/USGrantHomeGalena.jpg/440px-USGrantHomeGalena.jpg 2x" data-file-width="457" data-file-height="337" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USGrantHomeGalena.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The post-Civil War <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_Home" title="Ulysses S. Grant Home">home</a> of Ulysses S. Grant, in <a href="/wiki/Galena,_Illinois" title="Galena, Illinois">Galena, Illinois</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the war's end, Grant remained commander of the army, with duties that included enforcement of Reconstruction in the former Confederate states and supervision of Indian wars on the western Plains.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a410.E2.80.93411_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a410.E2.80.93411-135"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant secured a house for his family in Georgetown Heights in 1865, but instructed Elihu Washburne that for political purposes his legal residence remained in Galena, Illinois.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981232.E2.80.93233_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981232.E2.80.93233-136"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> That same year, Grant spoke at <a href="/wiki/Cooper_Union" title="Cooper Union">Cooper Union</a> in New York, where the <i>New York Times</i> reported that "...&#160;the entranced and bewildered multitude trembled with extraordinary delight." Further travels that summer took the Grants to <a href="/wiki/Albany,_New_York" title="Albany, New York">Albany, New York</a>, back to Galena, and throughout Illinois and Ohio, with enthusiastic receptions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981234_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981234-137"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In November 1865, Johnson sent Grant on a fact-finding mission to the South. Afterwards, Grant recommended continuation of a reformed <a href="/wiki/Freedmen%27s_Bureau" title="Freedmen's Bureau">Freedmen's Bureau</a>, which Johnson opposed, but advised against the use of black troops in garrisons, which he believed encouraged an alternative to farm labor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a390Smith420McFeely_1981238.E2.80.93241_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a390Smith420McFeely_1981238.E2.80.93241-138"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant did not believe the people of the devastated South were ready for civilian self-rule, and that both whites and blacks in the South required protection by the federal government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a390_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a390-139"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> He also warned of threats by disaffected poor people, black and white, and recommended that local decision-making be entrusted only to "thinking men of the South" (i.e., men of property).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981238.2C_240_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981238.2C_240-140"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> In this respect, Grant's opinion on Reconstruction aligned with Johnson's policy of pardoning established southern leaders and restoring them to their positions of power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981240Smith420_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981240Smith420-141"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> He joined Johnson in arguing that Congress should allow representatives from the South to take their seats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981240.E2.80.93241_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981240.E2.80.93241-142"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> On July 25, 1866, Congress promoted Grant to the newly created rank of <a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army_of_the_United_States" title="General of the Army of the United States" class="mw-redirect">General of the Army of the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith434n_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith434n-143"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breach_with_Johnson">Breach with Johnson</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg" class="image"><img alt=" Colored print of portrait of Grant surrounded by scenes from his military career" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg/220px-Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg/330px-Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg/440px-Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5626" data-file-height="7412" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_S._Grant_from_West_Point_to_Appomattox.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Clockwise from lower left: Graduated at West Point 1843; Chapultepec 1847; Drilling his Volunteers 1861; Fort Donelson 1862; Shiloh 1862; Vicksburg 1863; Chattanooga 1863; Commander-in-Chief 1864; Lee's Surrender 1865</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Johnson favored a lenient approach to Reconstruction, calling for an immediate return of the former Confederate states into the Union without any guarantee of African American civil rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a397.E2.80.93398_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a397.E2.80.93398-144"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Radical_Republican" title="Radical Republican">Radical Republican</a>-controlled Congress opposed the idea and refused to admit Congressmen from the former Confederate states.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a392.2C_396_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a392.2C_396-145"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> Over Johnson's vetoes, Congress renewed the Freedman's Bureau and passed the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1866" title="Civil Rights Act of 1866">Civil Rights Act of 1866</a>. During the congressional election campaign later that year, Johnson took his case to the people in his "<a href="/wiki/Swing_Around_the_Circle" title="Swing Around the Circle">Swing Around the Circle</a>" speaking tour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a396_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a396-146"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> Johnson pressured Grant, by then the most popular man in the country, to go on the tour; Grant, wishing to appear loyal, agreed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith369.E2.80.93397Brands_2012a389_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith369.E2.80.93397Brands_2012a389-147"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant believed that Johnson was purposefully agitating conservative opinion to defy Congressional Reconstruction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a397.E2.80.93398_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a397.E2.80.93398-144"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> Finding himself increasingly at odds with Johnson, Grant confided to his wife that he thought the President's speeches were a "national disgrace".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a397.E2.80.93398_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a397.E2.80.93398-144"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> Publicly, Grant attempted to appear loyal to Johnson while not alienating Republican legislators essential to his future political career. Concerned that Johnson's differences with Congress would cause renewed insurrection in the South, he ordered Southern arsenals to ship arms north to prevent their capture by Southern state governments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a398.E2.80.93399_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a398.E2.80.93399-148"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Conflict between radicals and conservatives continued after the 1866 congressional elections. Rejecting Johnson's vision for quick reconciliation with former Confederates, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Acts" title="Reconstruction Acts">Reconstruction Acts</a>, which divided the southern states into five military districts to protect the freedman's constitutional and congressional rights. Military district governors were to lead transitional state governments in each district. Grant, who was to select the general to govern each district from a group designated by Johnson, preferred Congress's plan for enforcement of Reconstruction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith432.E2.80.93433_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith432.E2.80.93433-149"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant was optimistic that Reconstruction Acts would help pacify the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith434.E2.80.93435_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith434.E2.80.93435-150"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> By complying with the Acts and instructing his subordinates to do likewise, Grant further alienated Johnson. When Sheridan removed public officials in Louisiana who impeded Reconstruction, Johnson was displeased and sought Sheridan's removal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith436.E2.80.93439_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith436.E2.80.93439-151"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant recommended a rebuke, but not a dismissal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981259.E2.80.93261_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981259.E2.80.93261-152"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> Throughout the Reconstruction period, Grant and the military protected the rights of more than 1,500 <a href="/wiki/List_of_African-American_officeholders_during_the_Reconstruction" title="List of African-American officeholders during the Reconstruction" class="mw-redirect">African Americans elected to political office</a> and overturned the first <a href="/wiki/Black_Codes_(United_States)" title="Black Codes (United States)">black codes</a> in 1867.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith421.2C_433_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith421.2C_433-153"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Johnson.27s_impeachment">Johnson's impeachment</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Senate-Johnson-Impeachment-Trials.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6a/Senate-Johnson-Impeachment-Trials.jpg/220px-Senate-Johnson-Impeachment-Trials.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6a/Senate-Johnson-Impeachment-Trials.jpg/330px-Senate-Johnson-Impeachment-Trials.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6a/Senate-Johnson-Impeachment-Trials.jpg/440px-Senate-Johnson-Impeachment-Trials.jpg 2x" data-file-width="610" data-file-height="404" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Senate-Johnson-Impeachment-Trials.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The trial of Andrew Johnson before the Senate</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Johnson wished to replace Stanton, a Lincoln appointee who sympathized with Congressional Reconstruction. To keep Grant under control as a potential political rival, Johnson asked him to take the post. Grant recommended against the move, in light of the <a href="/wiki/Tenure_of_Office_Act_(1867)" title="Tenure of Office Act (1867)">Tenure of Office Act</a>, which required Senate approval for cabinet removals. Johnson believed the Act did not apply to officers appointed by the previous president, and forced the issue by making Grant an interim appointee during a Senate recess. Grant agreed to accept the post temporarily, and Stanton vacated the office until the Senate reconvened.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981262.E2.80.93264_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981262.E2.80.93264-154"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When the Senate reinstated Stanton, Johnson told Grant to refuse to surrender the office and let the courts resolve the matter. Grant told Johnson in private that violating the Tenure of Office Act was a federal offense, which could result in a fine or imprisonment. Believing he had no other legal alternatives, Grant returned the office to Stanton. This incurred Johnson's wrath; at a cabinet meeting immediately afterwards, Johnson accused Grant of breaking his promise to remain Secretary of War. Grant disputed that he had ever made such a promise although cabinet members later testified he had done so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith448.E2.80.93451_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith448.E2.80.93451-155"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> Newspapers friendly to Johnson published a series of articles to discredit Grant over returning the War Department to Stanton, stating that Grant had been deceptive in the matter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith448.E2.80.93451_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith448.E2.80.93451-155"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> This public insult infuriated Grant, and he defended himself in an angry letter to Johnson, after which the two men were confirmed foes. When Grant's statement became public, it increased his popularity among Radical Republicans and he emerged from the controversy unscathed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith448.E2.80.93451_155-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith448.E2.80.93451-155"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Grant favored Johnson's impeachment, he took no active role in the <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_of_Andrew_Johnson" title="Impeachment of Andrew Johnson">impeachment proceedings against Johnson</a>, which were fueled in part by Johnson's removal of Stanton. Johnson barely survived, and none of the other Republican leaders directly involved benefited politically in their unsuccessful attempt to remove the president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981275_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981275-156"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1868">Election of 1868</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of crowd in front of Capitol building decorated with patriotic bunting" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg/220px-Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg/330px-Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg/440px-Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2745" data-file-height="1947" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Inauguration_of_Grant_-_NARA_-_530394.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First inauguration of Ulysses S. Grant on the steps of the Capitol on March 4, 1869.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1868" title="United States presidential election, 1868">United States presidential election, 1868</a></div>
<p>While remaining Commanding General, Grant entered the 1868 campaign season with increased popularity among the Radical Republicans following his abandonment of Johnson over the Secretary of War dispute. The Republicans chose Grant as their presidential candidate on the first ballot at the <a href="/wiki/1868_Republican_National_Convention" title="1868 Republican National Convention">1868 Republican National Convention</a> in Chicago. In his letter of acceptance to the party, Grant concluded with "Let us have peace", which became his campaign slogan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981264.E2.80.93267_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981264.E2.80.93267-157"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> For vice president, the delegates nominated House Speaker <a href="/wiki/Schuyler_Colfax" title="Schuyler Colfax">Schuyler Colfax</a>. Grant's 1862 <a href="/wiki/General_Order_No._11_(1862)" title="General Order No. 11 (1862)">General Order No. 11</a> became an issue during the <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1868" title="U.S. presidential election, 1868" class="mw-redirect">presidential campaign</a>; he sought to distance himself from the order, saying "I have no prejudice against sect or race, but want each individual to be judged by his own merit."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith459.E2.80.93460_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith459.E2.80.93460-158"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> As President, Grant would atone for 1862's <a href="/wiki/General_Order_No._11_(1862)" title="General Order No. 11 (1862)">expulsion of the Jews</a>. Historian <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Sarna" title="Jonathan Sarna">Jonathan Sarna</a> argues that Grant became one of the greatest friends of Jews in American history, meeting with them often and appointing them to high office. He was the first president to condemn atrocities against Jews in Europe, thus putting human rights on the American diplomatic agenda.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESarna_2012xi.2C_88-90.2C_101-3_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESarna_2012xi.2C_88-90.2C_101-3-159"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> As was expected at the time, Grant returned to <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_Home" title="Ulysses S. Grant Home">his home</a> state<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span>[</span>d<span>]</span></a></sup> and left the active campaigning and speaking on his behalf to his campaign manager <a href="/wiki/William_E._Chandler" title="William E. Chandler">William E. Chandler</a> and others.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981282.E2.80.93284_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981282.E2.80.93284-163"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> The Republican campaign focused on continuing Reconstruction and restoring the public credit. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner_2014340.2C_344_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner_2014340.2C_344-164"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Democrats nominated former New York Governor <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Seymour" title="Horatio Seymour">Horatio Seymour</a>. Their campaign focused mainly on ending Reconstruction and returning control of the South to the white planter class, which alienated many <a href="/wiki/War_Democrats" title="War Democrats">War Democrats</a> in the North.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith468.E2.80.93469_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith468.E2.80.93469-165"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> The Democrats attacked Reconstruction and the Republican Party's support of African American rights, while deriding Grant, calling him captain of the "Black Marines".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981278.2C_283_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981278.2C_283-166"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant won the election by 300,000 votes out of 5,716,082 votes cast, receiving an <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">electoral college</a> landslide, of 214 votes to Seymour's 80. Grant, at the age of 46 was (at the time) the youngest president ever elected. His election was a triumph of conservative principles that included sound money, efficient government, and the restoration of Southern reconstructed states.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith461_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith461-167"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant was the first president elected after the nation had outlawed slavery and granted citizenship to former slaves. Implementation of these new rights was slow to come; in the 1868 election, the black vote counted in only 16 of the 37 states, nearly all in the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981284_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981284-168"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant lost <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> primarily due to <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a> violence against African American voters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner_2014243.E2.80.93244_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner_2014243.E2.80.93244-169"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup> During the election there was a noticeably large number of black citizens in Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981287.E2.80.93288_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981287.E2.80.93288-170"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_1869.E2.80.9377">Presidency 1869–77</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Presidency of Ulysses S. Grant">Presidency of Ulysses S. Grant</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:473px;max-width:473px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_(cropped).tif" class="image"><img alt="Photographic portrait of Grant sitting by a table with open book and top hat on it" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-180px-Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg" width="180" height="230" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-270px-Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_%28cropped%29.tif/lossy-page1-360px-Ulysses_S._Grant_seated_by_Brady_%28cropped%29.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="850" data-file-height="1088" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">President Ulysses S. Grant, 1869</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:287px;max-width:287px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch,_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers,_NY,_1870.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Family group on porch" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch%2C_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers%2C_NY%2C_1870.jpg/285px-Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch%2C_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers%2C_NY%2C_1870.jpg" width="285" height="231" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch%2C_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers%2C_NY%2C_1870.jpg/428px-Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch%2C_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers%2C_NY%2C_1870.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch%2C_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers%2C_NY%2C_1870.jpg/570px-Ulysses_Grant_and_Family_at_Long_Branch%2C_NJ_by_Pach_Brothers%2C_NY%2C_1870.jpg 2x" data-file-width="823" data-file-height="668" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The First Family: Ulysses and Julia Grant's family at the "summer capital" in <a href="/wiki/Long_Branch,_New_Jersey" title="Long Branch, New Jersey">Long Branch, New Jersey</a>, 1870</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On March 4, 1869, Grant was sworn in as the eighteenth President of the United States by Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/Salmon_P._Chase" title="Salmon P. Chase">Salmon P. Chase</a>. His presidency began with a break from tradition, as Johnson did not attend Grant's inauguration at the Capitol or ride with him as he departed the White House for the last time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981287.E2.80.93288_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981287.E2.80.93288-170"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup> In his inaugural address, Grant urged the ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fifteenth Amendment</a> and said he would approach Reconstruction "calmly, without prejudice, hate or sectional pride."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatrick166_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatrick166-171"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant recommended the "proper treatment" of <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> be studied; advocating their civilization and eventual citizenship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981305_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981305-172"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant took an unconventional approach to choosing his cabinet, declining to consult with the Senate and keeping his choices secret until he submitted them for confirmation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith465.E2.80.93466McFeely_1981286.2C_290_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith465.E2.80.93466McFeely_1981286.2C_290-173"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> In his effort to create national harmony, Grant purposely avoided choosing Republican Party leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon_2002246.E2.80.93247_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon_2002246.E2.80.93247-174"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant appointed his wartime comrade John A. Rawlins as Secretary of War and <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Fish" title="Hamilton Fish">Hamilton Fish</a>, a conservative New York statesman, as Secretary of State. Sherman earned promotion to <a href="/wiki/Commanding_General_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Commanding General of the United States Army">Commanding General</a>, but his relationship with Grant became strained when the President took Rawlins's side when the Secretary of War sought to limit Sherman's authority. Rawlins died in office a few months later, and Grant appointed <a href="/wiki/William_W._Belknap" title="William W. Belknap">William W. Belknap</a> as his replacement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon_2002246.E2.80.93247_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon_2002246.E2.80.93247-174"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grant selected several non-politicians to his cabinet, including <a href="/wiki/Adolph_E._Borie" title="Adolph E. Borie">Adolph E. Borie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Turney_Stewart" title="Alexander Turney Stewart">Alexander Turney Stewart</a>, with limited success. Borie served briefly as Secretary of the Navy, later replaced by <a href="/wiki/George_M._Robeson" title="George M. Robeson">George M. Robeson</a>, while Stewart was prevented from becoming Secretary of the Treasury by a 1789 statute that barred businessmen from the position (Senators <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sumner" title="Charles Sumner">Charles Sumner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Conkling" title="Roscoe Conkling">Roscoe Conkling</a> opposed amending the law.)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981293.E2.80.93294_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981293.E2.80.93294-175"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup> In place of Stewart, Grant appointed Massachusetts Representative <a href="/wiki/George_S._Boutwell" title="George S. Boutwell">George S. Boutwell</a>, a radical, as Treasury Secretary. Grant's other cabinet appointments—<a href="/wiki/Jacob_D._Cox" title="Jacob D. Cox" class="mw-redirect">Jacob D. Cox</a> (Interior), <a href="/wiki/John_Creswell" title="John Creswell">John Creswell</a> (Postmaster General), and <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Rockwood_Hoar" title="Ebenezer Rockwood Hoar" class="mw-redirect">Ebenezer Rockwood Hoar</a> (Attorney General)—were well-received and uncontroversial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith469.E2.80.93470_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith469.E2.80.93470-176"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant also appointed four Justices to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a>: <a href="/wiki/William_Strong_(judge)" title="William Strong (judge)" class="mw-redirect">William Strong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_P._Bradley" title="Joseph P. Bradley">Joseph P. Bradley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ward_Hunt" title="Ward Hunt">Ward Hunt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/Morrison_Waite" title="Morrison Waite">Morrison Waite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith507.E2.80.93508.2C_562.E2.80.93563_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith507.E2.80.93508.2C_562.E2.80.93563-177"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> Hunt voted to uphold Reconstruction laws while Waite did much to undermine them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981387.E2.80.93389_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981387.E2.80.93389-178"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_Reconstruction_and_civil_rights">Later Reconstruction and civil rights</span></h3>
<p>Reconstruction of the South continued as Grant took office in March 1869. Grant, unlike his predecessor, advocated systematic federal enforcement of fundamental civil rights regardless of race.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaczorowski155_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaczorowski155-179"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> He lobbied Congress to pass the Fifteenth Amendment, guaranteeing that no state could prevent someone from voting based on race, and believed that its passage would secure freedmen's rights. Grant asked Congress to admit representatives from the remaining unrepresented Southern states in conformity with Congressional Reconstruction; they did so, passing legislation providing that Mississippi, Virginia, and Texas would be represented in Congress after they ratified the Fifteenth Amendment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a435_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a435-180"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant pressured Congress to draw up legislation that would seat African American state legislators in Georgia, who had been ousted by white conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a465_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a465-181"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress responded through special legislation; the members were re-seated in the Georgia legislature, and Georgia was required to adopt the Fifteenth Amendment to gain representation in Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a465_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a465-181"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> By July 1870, the four remaining states were readmitted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a465_181-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a465-181"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg/220px-Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg/330px-Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg/440px-Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="961" data-file-height="740" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harpers1874LouisianaOutrage.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
White Leaguers attacking the integrated police force and state militia, New Orleans, September 1874</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>To enforce the new amendment, Grant relied on the army, as well as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Justice_Department" title="United States Justice Department" class="mw-redirect">Justice Department</a>, created in 1870 to enforce federal laws in the South. Where the attorney general had once been only a legal adviser to the president, he now led a cabinet department dedicated to enforcing federal law, including a <a href="/wiki/Solicitor_General_of_the_United_States" title="Solicitor General of the United States">solicitor general</a> to argue on the government's behalf in court.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith543.E2.80.93545_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith543.E2.80.93545-182"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> Under Grant's first attorney general, <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_R._Hoar" title="Ebenezer R. Hoar">Ebenezer R. Hoar</a>, the administration was not especially aggressive in prosecuting white Southerners who terrorized their black neighbors, but Hoar's successor, <a href="/wiki/Amos_T._Akerman" title="Amos T. Akerman">Amos T. Akerman</a>, was more zealous. Alarmed by a rise in terror by the <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a> and other groups against <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans" class="mw-redirect">African Americans</a>, Congress (with Grant's encouragement) passed a series of three laws, the <a href="/wiki/Enforcement_Acts" title="Enforcement Acts">Enforcement Acts</a> from 1870 to 1871, which made depriving African Americans their civil rights a federal offense and authorized the president to use the military to enforce the laws.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981368.E2.80.93369Brands_2012a467.2C_470_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981368.E2.80.93369Brands_2012a467.2C_470-183"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup> In May 1871, Grant ordered federal troops to help <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marshals_Service" title="United States Marshals Service">marshals</a> in arresting Klansmen. That October, on Akerman's recommendation, Grant suspended <i><a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a></i> in part of South Carolina and sent federal troops to enforce the law there. After prosecutions by Akerman and his replacement, <a href="/wiki/George_Henry_Williams" title="George Henry Williams">George Henry Williams</a>, the Klan's power collapsed; by 1872, elections in the South saw African Americans voting in record numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith544.E2.80.93547_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith544.E2.80.93547-184"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup> That same year, Grant signed the <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_Act" title="Amnesty Act">Amnesty Act</a>, which restored political rights to former Confederates. Lacking sufficient funding, the Justice Department stopped prosecutions of the Klan in June 1873, and Grant offered the Klan clemency in exchange for peace.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaczorowski182_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaczorowski182-185"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After the Klan's decline, other conservative whites formed armed groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Red_Shirts_(Southern_United_States)" title="Red Shirts (Southern United States)">Red Shirts</a> and the <a href="/wiki/White_League" title="White League">White League</a> who openly used violence and intimidation to take control of state governments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERable144.E2.80.93186_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERable144.E2.80.93186-186"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1873" title="Panic of 1873">Panic of 1873</a> and the ensuing depression contributed to public fatigue, and the North grew less concerned with Reconstruction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith552.E2.80.93553_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith552.E2.80.93553-187"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup> Supreme Court rulings in the <i><a href="/wiki/Slaughter-House_Cases" title="Slaughter-House Cases">Slaughter-House Cases</a></i> (1873) and <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Cruikshank" title="United States v. Cruikshank">United States v. Cruikshank</a></i> (1875) restricted federal enforcement of civil rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaczorowski184_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaczorowski184-188"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant began to favor limiting the use of troops, to avoid the impression that he was acting as a military dictator; he was also concerned that increased military pressure in the South might cause conservative whites in the North to bolt the Republican Party. In 1874, Grant by proclamation ended the <a href="/wiki/Brooks%E2%80%93Baxter_War" title="Brooks–Baxter War">Brooks–Baxter War</a> bringing Reconstruction in <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> to a peaceful conclusion, but that same year, he sent troops and warships under Major General <a href="/wiki/William_H._Emory" title="William H. Emory">William H. Emory</a> to New Orleans in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Colfax_Massacre" title="Colfax Massacre" class="mw-redirect">Colfax Massacre</a> and disputes over the election of Governor <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_Kellogg" title="William Pitt Kellogg">William Pitt Kellogg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a538.E2.80.93541Foner_2014528_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a538.E2.80.93541Foner_2014528-189"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup> Emory peacefully restored Kellogg to office and the following year the parties reached a compromise allowing Democrats to retain control of the Louisiana House.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENystrom176Foner_2014555_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENystrom176Foner_2014555-190"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> Under public pressure Grant recalled General Sheridan and most of the federal troops from Louisiana.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a553_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a553-191"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By 1875, Democratic "<a href="/wiki/Redeemers" title="Redeemers">Redeemer</a>" politicians took control of all but three Southern states. As violence against black Southerners escalated once more, <a href="/wiki/Edwards_Pierrepont" title="Edwards Pierrepont">Edwards Pierrepont</a> (Grant's fourth attorney general) told Governor <a href="/wiki/Adelbert_Ames" title="Adelbert Ames">Adelbert Ames</a> of Mississippi that the people were "tired of the autumnal outbreaks in the South", and declined to intervene directly, instead, sending an emissary to negotiate a peaceful election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981420.E2.80.93422_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981420.E2.80.93422-192"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant signed an ambitious <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1875" title="Civil Rights Act of 1875">Civil Rights Act of 1875</a>, which expanded federal law enforcement by prohibiting discrimination on account of race in <a href="/wiki/Public_accommodations" title="Public accommodations">public lodging</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_transport" title="Public transport">public transportation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jury_selection_in_the_United_States" title="Jury selection in the United States">jury service</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a553_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a553-191"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> However, it did not stop the rise of white supremacist forces in the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981418.E2.80.93419_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981418.E2.80.93419-193"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> In October 1876, Grant sent troops to South Carolina to aid Republican Governor <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Henry_Chamberlain" title="Daniel Henry Chamberlain">Daniel Chamberlain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a570_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a570-194"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> Even so, the remaining three Republican governments in the South fell to Redeemers after the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1876" title="United States presidential election, 1876">1876 presidential election</a>, and the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1877" title="Compromise of 1877">Compromise of 1877</a> marked the end of Reconstruction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith603.E2.80.93604_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith603.E2.80.93604-195"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196"><span>[</span>e<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_peace_policy">Indian peace policy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ely_S._Parker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Formal photographic portrait of a sitting mustached man" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ely_S._Parker.jpg/220px-Ely_S._Parker.jpg" width="220" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ely_S._Parker.jpg/330px-Ely_S._Parker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ely_S._Parker.jpg/440px-Ely_S._Parker.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1175" data-file-height="1421" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ely_S._Parker.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ely_S._Parker" title="Ely S. Parker">Ely S. Parker</a>, appointed by Grant, was the first <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> to serve as <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Indian_Affairs" title="Bureau of Indian Affairs">Commissioner of Indian Affairs</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Grant's attempts to live peacefully with <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> marked a radical reversal of what had since the 1830s been the government's policy of <a href="/wiki/Indian_removal" title="Indian removal">Indian removal</a>. He appointed <a href="/wiki/Ely_S._Parker" title="Ely S. Parker">Ely S. Parker</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Seneca_people" title="Seneca people">Seneca</a> Indian and member of his wartime staff, as <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Indian_Affairs" title="Bureau of Indian Affairs">Commissioner of Indian Affairs</a>. "My efforts in the future will be directed," Grant said in his second inaugural address, "by a humane course, to bring the aborigines of the country under the benign influences of education and civilization&#160;... Wars of extermination&#160;... are demoralizing and wicked." Grant's "Peace Policy" aimed to replace entrepreneurs serving as Indian agents with missionaries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981308.E2.80.93309Brands_2012a502_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981308.E2.80.93309Brands_2012a502-197"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1869, Grant signed a law establishing a <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Indian_Commissioners" title="Board of Indian Commissioners">Board of Indian Commissioners</a> to oversee spending and reduce corruption in the Bureau of Indian Affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith535_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith535-198"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup> Two years later, he signed a bill ending the Indian treaty system; the law now treated individual Native Americans as <a href="/wiki/Ward_(law)" title="Ward (law)">wards</a> of the federal government, and no longer dealt with the tribes as sovereign entities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaltmann327_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaltmann327-199"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant wished for Indian tribes to be protected on reservations and educated in European-style farming and culture, abandoning their hunter-gatherer way of life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981308.E2.80.93309Brands_2012a502_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981308.E2.80.93309Brands_2012a502-197"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> Although, as biographer Jean Edward Smith wrote, Grant's peace policy was "remarkably progressive and humanitarian" for its time, it ultimately disregarded native cultures, something modern Americans see "as a grave error."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith541_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith541-200"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The peace policy showed some success in reducing battles between Indians and whites on the western frontier, but the increased slaughter of the buffalo, encouraged by Grant's subordinates, led to conflict with the <a href="/wiki/Plains_Indians" title="Plains Indians">Plains Indians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith536.E2.80.93538Brands_2012a501.E2.80.93503_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith536.E2.80.93538Brands_2012a501.E2.80.93503-201"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sioux" title="Sioux">Sioux</a> and other Plains tribes accepted the reservation system, but encroachments by whites in search of <a href="/wiki/Black_Hills_Gold_Rush" title="Black Hills Gold Rush">gold in the Black Hills</a> led to <a href="/wiki/Great_Sioux_War_of_1876" title="Great Sioux War of 1876">renewed war</a> by the end of Grant's second term, ending the understanding that had developed between Grant and Sioux Chief <a href="/wiki/Red_Cloud" title="Red Cloud">Red Cloud</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a501.E2.80.93503McFeely_1981436.E2.80.93437_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a501.E2.80.93503McFeely_1981436.E2.80.93437-202"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> Under Major Generals <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Otis_Howard" title="Oliver Otis Howard" class="mw-redirect">Oliver Otis Howard</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Crook" title="George Crook">George Crook</a>, Grant's policy had greater success in the Southwest. Howard, the former head of the Freedmen's Bureau, negotiated peace with the <a href="/wiki/Apache" title="Apache">Apache</a> in 1872, convincing their leader, <a href="/wiki/Cochise" title="Cochise">Cochise</a>, to move the tribe to a new reservation, and ending <a href="/wiki/Apache_Wars" title="Apache Wars">a war</a> started the year before. In Oregon, relations were less peaceful, as <a href="/wiki/Modoc_War" title="Modoc War">war with the Modocs</a> erupted in April 1873. The <a href="/wiki/Modoc_people" title="Modoc people">Modocs</a> refused to move to a reservation and killed the local army commander, Major General <a href="/wiki/Edward_Canby" title="Edward Canby">Edward Canby</a>. Although Grant was upset over Canby's death, he ordered restraint, disregarding Sherman's advice to seek revenge or exterminate the tribe. The army captured, tried, and executed the four Modoc warriors responsible for Canby's murder in October 1873. Grant ordered the rest of the Modoc tribe relocated to the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Territory" title="Indian Territory">Indian Territory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith532.E2.80.93535_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith532.E2.80.93535-203"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Great_Sioux_War" title="Great Sioux War" class="mw-redirect">Great Sioux War</a>, fueled by the discovery of gold in the Black Hills, Grant came into conflict with Colonel <a href="/wiki/George_Armstrong_Custer" title="George Armstrong Custer">George Armstrong Custer</a> after Custer testified in 1876 about corruption in the War Department. Grant ordered Custer arrested for breach of military protocol and barred him from leading an upcoming campaign against the Sioux. Grant later relented and let Custer fight under Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Terry" title="Alfred Terry">Alfred Terry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonovan115.2C_322.E2.80.93323_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonovan115.2C_322.E2.80.93323-204"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup> Sioux warriors led by <a href="/wiki/Crazy_Horse" title="Crazy Horse">Crazy Horse</a> killed Custer at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Little_Big_Horn" title="Battle of the Little Big Horn" class="mw-redirect">Battle of the Little Big Horn</a>, the army's most famous defeat in the Indian wars. Two months later, Grant castigated Custer in the press, saying "I regard Custer's massacre as a sacrifice of troops, brought on by Custer himself, that was wholly unnecessary – wholly unnecessary."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a565.E2.80.93566_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a565.E2.80.93566-205"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup> Custer's death shocked the nation, leading Congress to appropriate funds for more troops, two more Western forts and barred Indians from purchasing weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonovan115.2C_322.E2.80.93323_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonovan115.2C_322.E2.80.93323-204"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_affairs">Foreign affairs</span></h3>
<table class="mw-stack" role="presentation" cellspacing="1" cellpadding="0" style="float:left; background:transparent; margin:0; padding:0;">
<tr>
<td>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Formal photographic portrait of bearded man" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg/170px-Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg" width="170" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg/255px-Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg/340px-Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1094" data-file-height="1506" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_Fish_Brady_Edited.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Fish" title="Hamilton Fish">Hamilton Fish</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Even before Grant became president, expansionists in American politics desired control over the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_islands" title="Caribbean islands" class="mw-redirect">Caribbean islands</a>. Andrew Johnson had recommended annexation but the early anti-imperialist Republicans in Congress rejected the plan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981336.E2.80.93338Brands_2012a452_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981336.E2.80.93338Brands_2012a452-206"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant renewed negotiations to <a href="/wiki/Annexation_of_Santo_Domingo" title="Annexation of Santo Domingo">annex the Dominican Republic</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Orville_E._Babcock" title="Orville E. Babcock">Orville E. Babcock</a>, a wartime confidant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981336.E2.80.93338_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981336.E2.80.93338-207"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant was initially skeptical, but at the urging of Admiral Porter, who wanted a naval base at <a href="/wiki/Saman%C3%A1_Bay" title="Samaná Bay">Samaná Bay</a>, and Joseph W. Fabens, a New England businessman employed by the Dominican government, Grant became convinced of the plan's merit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith500.E2.80.93502_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith500.E2.80.93502-208"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup> He sent Babcock to consult with <a href="/wiki/Buenaventura_B%C3%A1ez" title="Buenaventura Báez">Buenaventura Báez</a>, who was then the Dominican president and supported annexation. Babcock returned with a draft treaty for annexation in December 1869.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith500.E2.80.93502_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith500.E2.80.93502-208"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grant believed in peaceful expansion of the nation's borders, and thought acquisition of the island nation - whose demographic featured a black majority - would allow new economic opportunities for African Americans in the United States while increasing American naval power in the Caribbean.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a455.E2.80.93456_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a455.E2.80.93456-209"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> Secretary of State Hamilton Fish dismissed the idea, seeing the island as politically unstable and troublesome.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith500.E2.80.93502_208-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith500.E2.80.93502-208"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant personally lobbied Senators to pass the treaty, going so far as to visit Sumner at his home.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981339.E2.80.93341_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981339.E2.80.93341-210"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> Fish added to the effort out of loyalty to the administration, but to no avail; the Senate refused to pass the treaty. Sumner's role in leading opposition toward annexation led to political enmity between him and Grant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981349.E2.80.93352_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981349.E2.80.93352-211"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> After the Dominican initiative failed, Grant convinced Fish to stay in the cabinet and gave him greater authority to run the State Department.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a454_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a454-212"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup> Unwilling to admit defeat, Grant successfully lobbied Congress to send a commission to the West Indies to investigate, including <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Douglass" title="Frederick Douglass">Frederick Douglass</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a461_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a461-213"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Douglass and the commission approved of Grant's claims for annexation in its findings released on April 5, 1871, the Senate remained opposed while Grant was forced to abandon further annexation attempts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a461Smith505-506_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a461Smith505-506-214"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grant and Fish were more successful in their resolution of the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Claims" title="Alabama Claims"><i>Alabama</i> claims</a>. This dispute between the United Kingdom and the United States stemmed from the damage done to American shipping during the Civil War by the five ships built for the Confederacy in British shipyards including, most famously, the <a href="/wiki/CSS_Alabama" title="CSS Alabama">CSS&#160;<i>Alabama</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981334.E2.80.93336_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981334.E2.80.93336-215"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup> The Americans claimed that Britain had violated neutrality by building ships for the Confederate Navy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon_2002249_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon_2002249-216"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup> When the war ended, the United States demanded restitution, which the British refused to pay. Negotiations continued fitfully, a sticking point being the claims of "indirect damages" on top of the harm directly caused by the five ships.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981352.E2.80.93354_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981352.E2.80.93354-217"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup> Sumner opposed the Johnson administration's proposed settlement, which had been rejected by the Senate, believing that Britain should directly pay $2 billion in gold or, alternatively, cede Canada to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith508_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith508-218"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup> Fish convinced Grant that peaceful relations with Britain were more important than acquisition of more territory, and the two nations agreed to negotiate along those lines.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith509.E2.80.93511_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith509.E2.80.93511-219"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup> A commission in Washington produced a treaty whereby an international tribunal would settle the damage amounts; the British admitted regret, but not fault.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221"><span>[</span>f<span>]</span></a></sup> The Senate approved the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Washington_(1871)" title="Treaty of Washington (1871)">Treaty of Washington</a>, which also settled disputes over fishing rights and maritime boundaries, by a 50–12 vote in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith512.E2.80.93515_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith512.E2.80.93515-220"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1873, a Spanish cruiser took captive a merchant ship, <a href="/wiki/Virginius_Affair" title="Virginius Affair"><i>Virginius</i></a>, flying the U.S. flag, carrying <a href="/wiki/Materiel" title="Materiel">war materials</a> and men to aid the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Years%27_War" title="Ten Years' War">Cuban insurrection</a>. The passengers and crew, including eight American citizens, were illegally traveling to Cuba to help overthrow the government. Spanish authorities executed the prisoners, and many Americans called for war with Spain. Fish, with Grant's support, worked to reach a peaceful resolution. Spain's President, <a href="/wiki/Emilio_Castelar_y_Ripoll" title="Emilio Castelar y Ripoll">Emilio Castelar y Ripoll</a>, expressed regret for the tragedy and agreed to decide reparations through arbitration; Spain surrendered the <i>Virginius</i> and paid a cash indemnity of $80,000 to the families of the executed Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENevins667.E2.80.93694_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENevins667.E2.80.93694-222"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup> In June 1874, Grant's Secretary of the Navy, George M. Robeson, commissioned the reconstruction of five redesigned double-turreted <a href="/wiki/Monitor_(warship)" title="Monitor (warship)">monitor</a> warships to compete with the superior Spanish Navy. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriedman405.E2.80.93406_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriedman405.E2.80.93406-223"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup> The administration's diplomacy was also at work in the Pacific as, in December 1874, Grant held a state dinner at the White House for the King of Hawaii, <a href="/wiki/David_Kalakaua" title="David Kalakaua" class="mw-redirect">David Kalakaua</a>, who was seeking duty-free sugar importation to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKreiser19_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKreiser19-224"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant and Fish were able to produce a successful free trade treaty in 1875 with the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hawaii" title="Kingdom of Hawaii">Kingdom of Hawaii</a>, incorporating the Pacific islands' sugar industry into the United States' economic sphere.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKreiser19_224-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKreiser19-224"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gold_standard_and_the_Gold_Ring">Gold standard and the Gold Ring</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif" class="image"><img alt="Cartoon, entitled &quot;The 'Boy of the Period' Stirring up the Animals', shows Fisk poking bulls and bears and a man running toward them with a bag in his hand" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5764" data-file-height="4062" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Thwarting_the_Gold_Ring_1869.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant running from the Treasury to release gold into the market and defeat the Gold Ring</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Soon after taking office, Grant took steps to return the nation's currency to a more secure footing. During the Civil War, Congress had authorized the Treasury to issue banknotes that, unlike the rest of the currency, were not backed by gold or silver. The "<a href="/wiki/United_States_Note" title="United States Note">greenback</a>" notes, as they were known, were necessary to pay the unprecedented war debts, but they also caused inflation and forced gold-backed money out of circulation; Grant determined to return the national economy to pre-war monetary standards.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981279_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981279-225"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup> Many in Congress agreed, and they passed the <a href="/wiki/Public_Credit_Act_of_1869" title="Public Credit Act of 1869">Public Credit Act of 1869</a>, which guaranteed that bondholders would be repaid in gold, not greenbacks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith480.E2.80.93481_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith480.E2.80.93481-226"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup> To strengthen the dollar, Treasury Secretary George S. Boutwell, backed by Grant, sold gold from the Treasury each month and bought back high-interest Treasury bonds issued during the war; this had the effect of reducing the deficit, but deflating the currency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith480.E2.80.93481_226-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith480.E2.80.93481-226"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>These actions had a large impact on the nation's small gold market.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith482_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith482-227"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abel_Corbin" title="Abel Corbin">Abel Corbin</a>, Grant's brother-in-law, used his connection with the president to gain inside information for himself and his associates: <a href="/wiki/Jay_Gould" title="Jay Gould">Jay Gould</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a> trader and railroad magnate, and <a href="/wiki/James_Fisk_(financier)" title="James Fisk (financier)">Jim Fisk</a> (the collaborators were later known as the "Gold Ring.")<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a437.E2.80.93443_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a437.E2.80.93443-228"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup> Corbin convinced Grant to appoint a Gould associate as assistant Treasurer, where he could gather information for the Ring.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981323.E2.80.93324_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981323.E2.80.93324-229"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> Meanwhile, Gould and Fisk quietly stockpiled gold. Gould convinced Corbin that a high gold price would be good for the nation's prosperity, and Corbin passed this theory on to Grant, who appeared to be convinced. After consulting with Alexander Stewart (his erstwhile nominee for Treasury Secretary) in early September, Grant stopped the sale of gold, believing a higher gold price would help Western farmers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a437.E2.80.93443_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a437.E2.80.93443-228"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup> By mid-September, however, Grant became suspicious of Corbin. The gold price continued to rise as the conspirators bought ever more, and the rising price affected the wider economy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith486.E2.80.93489_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith486.E2.80.93489-230"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant, seeing that the increase was unnatural, told Boutwell to sell gold, thereby reducing its price.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith486.E2.80.93489_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith486.E2.80.93489-230"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup> Boutwell did so the next day, on September 22, 1869, later known as <a href="/wiki/Black_Friday_(1869)" title="Black Friday (1869)">Black Friday</a>. The sale of gold from the Treasury defeated Gould's scheme as the gold price plummeted, relieving the growing economic tension.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a445.2C_636_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a445.2C_636-231"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup> Gould and Fisk managed to escape without much harm to themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith490_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith490-232"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup> Many brokerage firms collapsed while trade volume and agriculture prices plummeted, causing a mild recession, but by January 1870, the economy resumed its post-war recovery. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981328Smith490_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981328Smith490-233"><span>[</span>227<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1872">Election of 1872</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1872" title="United States presidential election, 1872">United States presidential election, 1872</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A Thomas Nast cartoon depicting Grant steering a ship and being challenged by opponents during presidential election of 1872." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg/220px-Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg" width="220" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg/330px-Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg/440px-Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="1097" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Liberal_Gratitude.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cartoon by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Nast" title="Thomas Nast">Thomas Nast</a> on Grant's opponents in the reelection campaign</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite his administration's many scandals, Grant continued to be personally popular.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner_2014499.E2.80.93500_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner_2014499.E2.80.93500-234"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup> A growing number of reformers, however, were disappointed by Grant's support of Reconstruction, the Gold Ring, and <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_presidential_administration_scandals#New_York_custom_house_ring" title="Ulysses S. Grant presidential administration scandals">corruption in the New York Customs House</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner_2014500.E2.80.93507_235-0" class="reference"><a
