href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner_2014500.E2.80.93507-235"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cr%C3%A9dit_Mobilier_of_America_scandal" title="Crédit Mobilier of America scandal">Crédit Mobilier scandal</a>, in which a railroad company bribed many members of Congress in 1868, did not involve Grant, but did ensnare Vice President Colfax and added to the general sense of dishonesty in Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a508.E2.80.93509_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a508.E2.80.93509-236"><span>[</span>230<span>]</span></a></sup> To placate reformers, Grant created a <a href="/wiki/Civil_Service_Commission" title="Civil Service Commission" class="mw-redirect">Civil Service Commission</a> authorized by Congress in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatrick172_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatrick172-237"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup> The Commission, chaired by reformer <a href="/wiki/George_William_Curtis" title="George William Curtis">George William Curtis</a>, proposed certain reform rules and regulations, which Grant implemented by executive order in April 1872.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon_2002250_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon_2002250-238"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress refused to fund the Civil Service Commission in 1875 or pass legislation to implement its recommendations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith589.E2.80.93590Patrick173_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith589.E2.80.93590Patrick173-239"><span>[</span>233<span>]</span></a></sup> There was further division within the party between the faction most concerned with the plight of the freedmen and that concerned with the growth of industry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a488.E2.80.93489_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a488.E2.80.93489-240"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup> During the war, both factions' interests had aligned, and in 1868 both had supported Grant. As the wartime coalition began to fray, Grant's alignment with the party's pro-Reconstruction elements alienated party leaders who favored an end to federal intervention in Southern racial issues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a488.E2.80.93489_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a488.E2.80.93489-240"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many of that faction split from the party in 1872, calling themselves the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Liberal Republican Party (United States)">Liberal Republican Party</a>. Led by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Francis_Adams_Sr." title="Charles Francis Adams Sr." class="mw-redirect">Charles Francis Adams</a> of Massachusetts and Senator <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schurz" title="Carl Schurz">Carl Schurz</a> of Missouri, they publicly denounced the political patronage system that Sumner called "<a href="/wiki/Grantism" title="Grantism">Grantism</a>" and demanded amnesty for Confederate soldiers. The Liberal Republicans nominated <a href="/wiki/Horace_Greeley" title="Horace Greeley">Horace Greeley</a>, another Republican who had come to dislike Grant and his policies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a494_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a494-241"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup> The rest of the Republican Party nominated Grant for reelection, with Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wilson" title="Henry Wilson">Henry Wilson</a> of Massachusetts replacing Colfax as vice-presidential nominee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981381_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981381-242"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup> The Democrats, seeking to benefit from anti-Grant sentiment, nominated Greeley as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a495_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a495-243"><span>[</span>237<span>]</span></a></sup> To the Liberals' chagrin, Greeley made Grant's Southern policy, rather than reform, the main campaign issue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner_2014509_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner_2014509-244"><span>[</span>238<span>]</span></a></sup> The fusion effort failed and Grant was easily reelected. The Liberal Republicans were unable to deliver many votes, and Greeley was only successful in areas the Democrats would have carried without him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981384_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981384-245"><span>[</span>239<span>]</span></a></sup> A strong economy, debt reduction, lowered tariffs, repeal of the income tax, and civil service reforms helped Grant defeat the Liberal Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon_2002250_238-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon_2002250-238"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant won 56 percent of the popular vote and an Electoral College landslide of 286 to 66.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a499_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a499-246"><span>[</span>240<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247"><span>[</span>g<span>]</span></a></sup> A majority of <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans" class="mw-redirect">African Americans</a> in the South voted for Grant, while Democratic opposition remained mostly peaceful.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner_2014508_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner_2014508-248"><span>[</span>241<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Panic_of_1873.2C_the_Long_Depression.2C_and_currency_debates">Panic of 1873, the Long Depression, and currency debates</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A cartoon depicting Grant after vetoing the Inflation bill." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg/220px-Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg/330px-Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg/440px-Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="688" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Inflation_Bill_Veto.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant congratulated for vetoing the "inflation bill" on April 22, 1874. <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Fish" title="Hamilton Fish">Hamilton Fish</a>, shown behind Grant, supported the veto.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As his first term was ending, Grant continued to work for a strong dollar, signing into law the <a href="/wiki/Coinage_Act_of_1873" title="Coinage Act of 1873">Coinage Act of 1873</a>, which effectively ended the legal basis for <a href="/wiki/Bimetallism" title="Bimetallism">bimetallism</a> (the use of both silver and gold as money), and established the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> in practice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVenable66.E2.80.9368_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVenable66.E2.80.9368-249"><span>[</span>242<span>]</span></a></sup> The Coinage Act discontinued the <a href="/wiki/Seated_Liberty_dollar" title="Seated Liberty dollar">standard silver dollar</a> and established the <a href="/wiki/Gold_dollar" title="Gold dollar">gold dollar</a> as the sole monetary standard. Critics who wanted more money in circulation to facilitate easier credit later denounced the move as the "Crime of 1873", claiming the law caused deflation and helped bankers while hurting farmers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinstein307.E2.80.93326_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinstein307.E2.80.93326-250"><span>[</span>243<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grant's second term saw renewed economic turmoil. In September 1873, <a href="/wiki/Jay_Cooke_%26_Company" title="Jay Cooke &amp; Company">Jay Cooke &amp; Company</a>, a New York brokerage house, collapsed after it failed to sell all of the bonds issued by <a href="/wiki/Jay_Cooke" title="Jay Cooke">Cooke's</a> <a href="/wiki/Northern_Pacific_Railway" title="Northern Pacific Railway">Northern Pacific Railway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a517_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a517-251"><span>[</span>244<span>]</span></a></sup> The collapse sent ripples through Wall Street, and other banks and brokerages that owned railroad stocks and bonds were also ruined.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a517_251-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a517-251"><span>[</span>244<span>]</span></a></sup> On September 20, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a> suspended trading for ten days.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981393_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981393-252"><span>[</span>245<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant, who knew little about finance, traveled to New York to consult leading businessmen and bankers for advice on how to resolve the crisis, which became known as the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1873" title="Panic of 1873">Panic of 1873</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith576.E2.80.93579_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith576.E2.80.93579-253"><span>[</span>246<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant believed that, as with the collapse of the Gold Ring in 1869, the panic was merely an economic fluctuation that affected bankers and brokers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a518_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a518-254"><span>[</span>247<span>]</span></a></sup> He responded cautiously, instructing the treasury to buy $10 million in government bonds, thus injecting cash into the system. These purchases curbed the panic on Wall Street, but a five-year industrial depression, later called the <a href="/wiki/Long_Depression" title="Long Depression">Long Depression</a>, nonetheless swept the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith576.E2.80.93579_253-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith576.E2.80.93579-253"><span>[</span>246<span>]</span></a></sup> Many of the nation's railroads—89 out of 364—went bankrupt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981391Smith375.E2.80.93377_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981391Smith375.E2.80.93377-255"><span>[</span>248<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Congress hoped inflation would stimulate the economy and passed what became known as the Inflation Bill in 1874. Many farmers and workingmen favored the bill, which would have added $64 million in greenbacks to circulation, but some Eastern bankers opposed it because it would have weakened the dollar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981395_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981395-256"><span>[</span>249<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant unexpectedly vetoed the bill, saying that it would destroy the credit of the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith580.E2.80.93581_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith580.E2.80.93581-257"><span>[</span>250<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant's veto, supported by Fish, placed him securely in the conservative faction of the Republican party, and was the beginning of the party's commitment to a strong gold-backed dollar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith580.E2.80.93581_257-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith580.E2.80.93581-257"><span>[</span>250<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant later pressured Congress for a bill to further strengthen the dollar by gradually reducing the number of greenbacks in circulation. After losing the House to the Democrats in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1874" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1874">1874 elections</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lame_duck_(politics)" title="Lame duck (politics)">lame-duck</a> Republican Congress did so. On January 14, 1875, Grant signed the <a href="/wiki/Specie_Payment_Resumption_Act" title="Specie Payment Resumption Act">Specie Payment Resumption Act</a> into law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith580.E2.80.93581_257-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith580.E2.80.93581-257"><span>[</span>250<span>]</span></a></sup> The Resumption Act required gradual reduction of the amount of greenbacks allowed to circulate and declared that specie payment (i.e., in gold or silver) would resume in 1879.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith580.E2.80.93581_257-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith580.E2.80.93581-257"><span>[</span>250<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gilded_Age_corruption_and_reform">Gilded Age corruption and reform</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_presidential_administration_scandals" title="Ulysses S. Grant presidential administration scandals">Ulysses S. Grant presidential administration scandals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_presidential_administration_reforms" title="Ulysses S. Grant presidential administration reforms">Ulysses S. Grant presidential administration reforms</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Whiskeyring.jpg" class="image"><img alt="&quot;Uncle Sam&quot; cartoon tapping a Louisville whiskey barrel, captioned &quot;probe away&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Whiskeyring.jpg/220px-Whiskeyring.jpg" width="220" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Whiskeyring.jpg/330px-Whiskeyring.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Whiskeyring.jpg/440px-Whiskeyring.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="619" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Whiskeyring.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Weekly" title="Harper's Weekly">Harper's Weekly</a></i> cartoon on Bristow's Whiskey Ring investigation</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Grant served as president during the <a href="/wiki/Gilded_Age" title="Gilded Age">Gilded Age</a>, a time when the economy was open to speculation and western expansion that fueled corruption in government offices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodward.27.27passim.27.27_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodward.27.27passim.27.27-258"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup> Against the harsh public revelation of the <a href="/wiki/Credit_Mobilier_of_America_scandal" title="Credit Mobilier of America scandal" class="mw-redirect">Credit Mobilier of America scandal</a>, Grant faced charges of misconduct in nearly all federal departments, engaging his administration in constant conflict between corrupt associates and reformers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1974133.E2.80.93134_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1974133.E2.80.93134-259"><span>[</span>252<span>]</span></a></sup> Although personally honest with his own money matters, Grant had difficulty in spotting corruption in others.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith592McFeely_1981407.E2.80.93415_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith592McFeely_1981407.E2.80.93415-260"><span>[</span>253<span>]</span></a></sup> He was protective of associates, whose prosecutions he saw as unjust and shielded associates from attack at the cost of his own reputation, unless evidence of personal misconduct was overwhelming.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith587_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith587-261"><span>[</span>254<span>]</span></a></sup> No person linked any of the scandals together, except possibly Grant's personal secretary, Orville E. Babcock, who indirectly controlled many cabinet departments and delayed investigations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1974133.E2.80.93134_259-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1974133.E2.80.93134-259"><span>[</span>252<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodward.27.27passim.27.27_258-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodward.27.27passim.27.27-258"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In Grant's second term, a congressional investigation exposed corruption in the Treasury Department, in a case that would become known as the <a href="/wiki/Sanborn_incident" title="Sanborn incident">Sanborn incident</a>: Secretary <a href="/wiki/William_Adams_Richardson" title="William Adams Richardson">William Richardson</a> had hired John Sanborn (a friend of Congressman Benjamin Butler) as an independent tax collector on a percentage basis, also known as a <i>moiety</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith578_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith578-262"><span>[</span>255<span>]</span></a></sup> Treasury officials were then privately instructed not to press for payment, so that accounts would eventually become delinquent in taxes and Sanborn would get paid more when he "discovered" the accounts' delinquency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith578_262-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith578-262"><span>[</span>255<span>]</span></a></sup> The congressional committee report condemned Richardson for permitting the aggressive tax collection system Sanborn used, but did not attempt impeachment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1974147.E2.80.93148_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1974147.E2.80.93148-263"><span>[</span>256<span>]</span></a></sup> To quell the public outcry and prevent future fraud, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Moiety_Acts" title="Anti-Moiety Acts">Anti-Moiety Act</a>. Signed into law by Grant in 1874, it abolished the moiety system.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1974133.E2.80.93134_259-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1974133.E2.80.93134-259"><span>[</span>252<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1874, on becoming Treasury Secretary, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_H._Bristow" title="Benjamin H. Bristow" class="mw-redirect">Benjamin H. Bristow</a> began a series of reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a556.E2.80.93557_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a556.E2.80.93557-264"><span>[</span>257<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohn417_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn417-265"><span>[</span>258<span>]</span></a></sup> Finding that millions of dollars in revenue were missing,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a556.E2.80.93557_264-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a556.E2.80.93557-264"><span>[</span>257<span>]</span></a></sup> and having gotten Grant's endorsement in advance ("Let no guilty man escape"), Bristow obtained 238 indictments. These led to 110 convictions, and millions in tax dollars were restored to the Treasury.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohn417_265-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn417-265"><span>[</span>258<span>]</span></a></sup> When Bristow's investigation implicated Babcock as part of the Whiskey Ring, Grant became defensive, believing Babcock was the innocent victim of a witch hunt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith591.E2.80.93593_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith591.E2.80.93593-266"><span>[</span>259<span>]</span></a></sup> While denying immunity to minor Whiskey Ring conspirators, Grant worked to protect Babcock.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohn417_265-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn417-265"><span>[</span>258<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1876, Babcock was found not guilty at a trial in which Grant testified on his behalf through a deposition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohn417_265-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn417-265"><span>[</span>258<span>]</span></a></sup> After the trial, under public pressure, Grant dismissed Babcock from the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith591.E2.80.93593_266-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith591.E2.80.93593-266"><span>[</span>259<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268"><span>[</span>h<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant later pardoned several Ring members.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohn417_265-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn417-265"><span>[</span>258<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As the scandals increased, Congress, with the House now under Democratic control, began several investigations into corruption in the administration, the most notable of which dealt with <a href="/wiki/Trader_post_scandal" title="Trader post scandal">profiteering at western trading posts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a560.E2.80.93561_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a560.E2.80.93561-269"><span>[</span>261<span>]</span></a></sup> involving Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/William_W._Belknap" title="William W. Belknap">William W. Belknap</a> <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a560Donovan104_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a560Donovan104-270"><span>[</span>262<span>]</span></a></sup> which led to his resignation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a560.E2.80.93561_269-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a560.E2.80.93561-269"><span>[</span>261<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress also investigated and reprimanded Navy Secretary George M. Robeson in 1876 for receiving bribes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1974153_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1974153-271"><span>[</span>263<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grant's <a href="/wiki/United_States_Civil_Service_Commission" title="United States Civil Service Commission">Civil Service Commission</a> reforms had limited success, as his cabinet implemented a merit system that increased the number of qualified candidates and relied less on Congressional patronage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith589.E2.80.93590Simon_2002250_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith589.E2.80.93590Simon_2002250-272"><span>[</span>264<span>]</span></a></sup> Interior Secretary <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Delano" title="Columbus Delano">Columbus Delano</a>, however, exempted his department from competitive examinations, and Congress refused to enact permanent Civil Service reform.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith589_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith589-273"><span>[</span>265<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zachariah_Chandler" title="Zachariah Chandler">Zachariah Chandler</a>, who succeeded Delano, cleaned up corruption in the Interior Department.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon_2002250Patrick172_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon_2002250Patrick172-274"><span>[</span>266<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant appointed reformers Edwards Pierrepont and <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Jewell" title="Marshall Jewell">Marshall Jewell</a> as Attorney General and Postmaster General, respectively, who supported Bristow's investigations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith584.E2.80.93585Hesseltine366_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith584.E2.80.93585Hesseltine366-275"><span>[</span>267<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1875, Pierrepont cleaned up corruption among the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney" title="United States Attorney">United States Attorneys</a> and Marshals in the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHesseltine374_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHesseltine374-276"><span>[</span>268<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant suggested other reforms as well, including a proposal that states should offer free <a href="/wiki/State_school" title="State school">public schooling</a> to all children; he also endorsed the <a href="/wiki/Blaine_Amendment" title="Blaine Amendment">Blaine Amendment</a>, which would have forbidden government aid to schools with religious affiliations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith570.E2.80.93571_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith570.E2.80.93571-277"><span>[</span>269<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1876">Election of 1876</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1876" title="United States presidential election, 1876">United States presidential election, 1876</a></div>
<p>Even as Grant drew cheers at the <a href="/wiki/Centennial_Exposition" title="Centennial Exposition">Centennial Exposition</a> in 1876, the collected scandals of the last eight years, the country's weak economy, and the Democratic gains in the House led many in the Republican party to repudiate him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981440.E2.80.93441Patrick255_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981440.E2.80.93441Patrick255-278"><span>[</span>270<span>]</span></a></sup> Bristow was among the leading candidates to replace him, suggesting that a large faction desired an end to "Grantism" and feared that Grant would run for a third term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981440.E2.80.93441_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981440.E2.80.93441-279"><span>[</span>271<span>]</span></a></sup> Ultimately, Grant did not run, but Bristow was not the nominee either, as the convention settled on Governor <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="Rutherford B. Hayes">Rutherford B. Hayes</a> of Ohio, a reformer. The Democrats nominated Governor <a href="/wiki/Samuel_J._Tilden" title="Samuel J. Tilden">Samuel J. Tilden</a> of New York. Voting irregularities in three Southern states caused the election that year to remain undecided for several months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith598_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith598-280"><span>[</span>272<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant told Congress to settle the matter through legislation and assured both sides that he would not use the army to force a result, except to curb violence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith601.E2.80.93603_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith601.E2.80.93603-281"><span>[</span>273<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress formed an <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Commission_(United_States)" title="Electoral Commission (United States)">Electoral Commission</a> to decide the matter. The result was the <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1877" title="Compromise of 1877">Compromise of 1877</a>: the Electoral Commission ruled that the disputed votes belonged to Hayes, but the last troops were withdrawn from Southern capitals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith604_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith604-282"><span>[</span>274<span>]</span></a></sup> The Republicans had won, but Reconstruction was over.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith604_282-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith604-282"><span>[</span>274<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency">Post-presidency</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_standing_postwar.tif" class="image"><img alt="Grant is standing in a civilian dress suit holding a top hat." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Grant_standing_postwar.tif/lossy-page1-170px-Grant_standing_postwar.tif.jpg" width="170" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Grant_standing_postwar.tif/lossy-page1-255px-Grant_standing_postwar.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Grant_standing_postwar.tif/lossy-page1-340px-Grant_standing_postwar.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3748" data-file-height="5368" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_standing_postwar.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ulysses S. Grant, some time after the Civil War</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Post-presidential_life_of_Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Post-presidential life of Ulysses S. Grant" class="mw-redirect">Post-presidential life of Ulysses S. Grant</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_tour">World tour</span></h3>
<p>After leaving the White House, Grant and his family stayed with friends for two months, before setting out on a world tour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981448.E2.80.93449_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981448.E2.80.93449-283"><span>[</span>275<span>]</span></a></sup> The trip, which would last two years, began in <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> in May 1877, where enormous crowds greeted the ex-president and his entourage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981454.E2.80.93455_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981454.E2.80.93455-284"><span>[</span>276<span>]</span></a></sup> The Grants dined with <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> at <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a>, and Grant gave several speeches in London.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a581.E2.80.93583_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a581.E2.80.93583-285"><span>[</span>277<span>]</span></a></sup> After a tour on the continent, the Grants spent a few months with their daughter Nellie, who had married an Englishman and moved to that country several years before. Grant and his wife journeyed to France and Italy, spending Christmas 1877 aboard <a href="/wiki/USS_Vandalia_(1876)" title="USS Vandalia (1876)">USS <i>Vandalia</i></a>, a warship docked in Palermo.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981460.E2.80.93465_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981460.E2.80.93465-286"><span>[</span>278<span>]</span></a></sup> A winter sojourn in the Holy Land followed, and they visited Greece before returning to Italy and a meeting with <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_XIII" title="Pope Leo XIII">Pope Leo XIII</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981466.E2.80.93467_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981466.E2.80.93467-287"><span>[</span>279<span>]</span></a></sup> They toured Spain before moving on to Germany, where Grant discussed military matters with Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a>, telling him that in the final stages of the Civil War, the Union Army fought to preserve the nation and to "destroy slavery".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a585.E2.80.93586_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a585.E2.80.93586-288"><span>[</span>280<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Grants left from England by ship, sailing through the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> to India. They visited cities throughout the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">Raj</a>, welcomed by colonial officials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981471.E2.80.93473_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981471.E2.80.93473-289"><span>[</span>281<span>]</span></a></sup> After India, they toured <a href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma" class="mw-redirect">Burma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siam" title="Siam" class="mw-redirect">Siam</a> (where Grant met with King <a href="/wiki/Chulalongkorn" title="Chulalongkorn">Chulalongkorn</a>), <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cochinchina" title="Cochinchina">Cochinchina</a> (Vietnam).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a590.E2.80.93591_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a590.E2.80.93591-290"><span>[</span>282<span>]</span></a></sup> Traveling on to Hong Kong, Grant began to change his mind on the nature of colonization, believing that British rule was not "purely selfish" but also good for the colonial subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981471.E2.80.93473_289-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981471.E2.80.93473-289"><span>[</span>281<span>]</span></a></sup> Leaving Hong Kong, the Grants visited the cities of Canton, Shanghai, and Peking, China. He declined to ask for an interview with the <a href="/wiki/Guangxu_Emperor" title="Guangxu Emperor">Guangxu Emperor</a>, a child of seven, but did speak with the head of government, <a href="/wiki/Prince_Gong_(Qing_dynasty)" title="Prince Gong (Qing dynasty)">Prince Gong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Li_Hongzhang" title="Li Hongzhang">Li Hongzhang</a>, a leading general.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a591.E2.80.93592_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a591.E2.80.93592-291"><span>[</span>283<span>]</span></a></sup> They discussed China's dispute with Japan over the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a>, and Grant agreed to help bring the two sides to agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a593.E2.80.93594_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a593.E2.80.93594-292"><span>[</span>284<span>]</span></a></sup> After crossing over to Japan and meeting the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Meiji" title="Emperor Meiji">Emperor Meiji</a>, Grant convinced China to accept the Japanese annexation of the islands, and the two nations avoided war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith612n.E2.80.93613n_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith612n.E2.80.93613n-293"><span>[</span>285<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By then, the Grants had been gone two years, and were homesick. They crossed the Pacific and landed in San Francisco in September 1879, greeted by cheering crowds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith613_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith613-294"><span>[</span>286<span>]</span></a></sup> After a visit to <a href="/wiki/Yosemite_National_Park" title="Yosemite National Park">Yosemite Valley</a>, they returned at last to Philadelphia on December 16, 1879.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981477_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981477-295"><span>[</span>287<span>]</span></a></sup> The voyage around the world had captured popular imagination, and Republicans—especially those of the <a href="/wiki/Stalwart_(politics)" title="Stalwart (politics)">Stalwart</a> faction excluded from the Hayes administration—saw Grant in a new light.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith614.E2.80.93615_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith614.E2.80.93615-296"><span>[</span>288<span>]</span></a></sup> The Republican nomination for 1880 was wide open after Hayes forswore a second term and many Republicans thought that Grant was the man for the job.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith614.E2.80.93615_296-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith614.E2.80.93615-296"><span>[</span>288<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_term_attempt">Third term attempt</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif" class="image"><img alt="Grant, shown in a cartoon as an acrobat hanging from rings, holding up multiple politician/acrobats" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif.jpg" width="220" height="338" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="765" data-file-height="1177" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulysses_S._Grant_Trapeze_Cartoon_Keppler_Puck_1880.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Puck_(magazine)" title="Puck (magazine)">Puck</a></i>, a Democratic magazine, in 1880 lampooned Grant's support of "rings" of corruption among his associates.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1880_Republican_National_Convention" title="1880 Republican National Convention">1880 Republican National Convention</a></div>
<p>Stalwarts, led by Grant's old political ally, <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Conkling" title="Roscoe Conkling">Roscoe Conkling</a>, saw the ex-president's renewed popularity as a way for their faction to regain power. Opponents denounced the idea as a violation of the two-term rule that had been the norm since George Washington. Grant said nothing publicly, but he wanted the job and encouraged his men.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHesseltine432.E2.80.93439_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHesseltine432.E2.80.93439-297"><span>[</span>289<span>]</span></a></sup> Elihu B. Washburne urged him to run; Grant demurred, saying he would be happy for the Republicans to win with another candidate, though he preferred <a href="/wiki/James_G._Blaine" title="James G. Blaine">James G. Blaine</a> to <a href="/wiki/John_Sherman" title="John Sherman">John Sherman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a600.E2.80.93601_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a600.E2.80.93601-298"><span>[</span>290<span>]</span></a></sup> Even so, Conkling and <a href="/wiki/John_A._Logan" title="John A. Logan">John A. Logan</a> began to organize delegates in Grant's favor. When <a href="/wiki/1880_Republican_National_Convention" title="1880 Republican National Convention">the convention</a> convened in Chicago in June, there were more delegates pledged to Grant than to any other candidate, but he was still short of a majority vote to capture the nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a600.E2.80.93601_298-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a600.E2.80.93601-298"><span>[</span>290<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At the convention, Conkling nominated Grant with an elegant speech, the most famous line being: "When asked which state he hails from, our sole reply shall be, he hails from Appomattox and its famous apple tree."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a600.E2.80.93601_298-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a600.E2.80.93601-298"><span>[</span>290<span>]</span></a></sup> With 370 votes needed for nomination, the first ballot had Grant at 304, Blaine at 284, Sherman at 93, and the rest scattered to minor candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981479.E2.80.93481_299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981479.E2.80.93481-299"><span>[</span>291<span>]</span></a></sup> Subsequent ballots followed, with roughly the same result; neither Grant nor Blaine could win. After thirty-six ballots, Blaine's delegates deserted him and combined with those of other candidates to nominate a compromise candidate: Representative <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">James A. Garfield</a> of Ohio.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a602_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a602-300"><span>[</span>292<span>]</span></a></sup> The 306 votes Grant received on the last ballot was not enough to secure the nomination. A procedural motion made the vote unanimous for Garfield, who accepted the nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith617_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith617-301"><span>[</span>293<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grant gave speeches for Garfield, but declined to criticize the Democratic nominee, <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott_Hancock" title="Winfield Scott Hancock">Winfield Scott Hancock</a>, a general who had served under him in the Army of the Potomac.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a604.E2.80.93605_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a604.E2.80.93605-302"><span>[</span>294<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield won the popular vote by a narrow margin, but solidly won the Electoral College—214 to 155. After the election, Grant gave Garfield his public support, and pushed him to include Stalwarts in his administration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a607.E2.80.93609_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a607.E2.80.93609-303"><span>[</span>295<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_ventures">Business ventures</span></h3>
<p>Grant's world tour had been costly. When he returned to America, Grant had depleted most of his savings and needed to earn money and find a new home.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a611_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a611-304"><span>[</span>296<span>]</span></a></sup> Wealthy friends bought him a home on Manhattan's <a href="/wiki/Upper_East_Side" title="Upper East Side">Upper East Side</a>, and to make an income, Grant, Jay Gould, and former Mexican Finance Secretary <a href="/wiki/Mat%C3%ADas_Romero" title="Matías Romero">Matías Romero</a> chartered the Mexican Southern Railroad, with plans to build a railroad from Oaxaca to Mexico City.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981486.E2.80.93489_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981486.E2.80.93489-305"><span>[</span>297<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant urged <a href="/wiki/Chester_A._Arthur" title="Chester A. Arthur">Chester A. Arthur</a>, who had succeeded Garfield as president in 1881, to negotiate a free trade treaty with Mexico. Arthur and the Mexican government agreed, but the United States Senate rejected the treaty in 1883.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981486.E2.80.93489_305-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981486.E2.80.93489-305"><span>[</span>297<span>]</span></a></sup> The railroad was similarly unsuccessful, falling into bankruptcy the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981486.E2.80.93489_305-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981486.E2.80.93489-305"><span>[</span>297<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At the same time, Grant's son Ulysses Jr. had opened a Wall Street brokerage house with <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Ward" title="Ferdinand Ward">Ferdinand Ward</a>. Regarded as a rising star, Ward, and the firm, Grant &amp; Ward, were initially successful.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981488.E2.80.93491_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981488.E2.80.93491-306"><span>[</span>298<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1883, Grant joined the firm and invested $100,000 of his own money.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a619_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a619-307"><span>[</span>299<span>]</span></a></sup> Investors bought <a href="/wiki/Security_(finance)" title="Security (finance)">securities</a> through the firm, and Ward used the securities as collateral to borrow money to buy more securities. Grant &amp; Ward pledged that collateral to borrow more money to trade in securities on the firm's own account. The practice—called <a href="/wiki/Hypothecation" title="Hypothecation">hypothecation</a>—was legal and accepted; what was illegal was <i>rehypothecation</i>, the practice of pledging the same securities as collateral for multiple loans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981488.E2.80.93491_306-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981488.E2.80.93491-306"><span>[</span>298<span>]</span></a></sup> Ward, having colluded with the bank involved, did this for many of the firm's assets. When the trades went bad, multiple loans came due, all backed up by the same collateral. Historians agree that Grant was likely unaware of Ward's tactics, but it is unclear how much Buck Grant knew. In May 1884, enough investments went bad to convince Ward that the firm would soon be bankrupt. Ward told Grant of the impending failure, but suggested that this was a temporary shortfall.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a620.E2.80.93621_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a620.E2.80.93621-308"><span>[</span>300<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant approached businessman <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Vanderbilt" title="William Henry Vanderbilt">William Henry Vanderbilt</a>, who gave him a personal loan of $150,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981492.E2.80.93493_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981492.E2.80.93493-309"><span>[</span>301<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant invested the money in the firm, but it was not enough to save the firm from failure. Essentially penniless, but compelled by a sense of personal honor, he repaid what he could with his Civil War mementos and the sale or transfer of all other assets.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerryxxix_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerryxxix-310"><span>[</span>302<span>]</span></a></sup> Although the proceeds did not cover the loan, Vanderbilt insisted the debt had been paid in full. Grant was left destitute.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a620.E2.80.93621_308-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a620.E2.80.93621-308"><span>[</span>300<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memoirs_and_death">Memoirs and death</span></h3>
<table class="mw-stack" role="presentation" cellspacing="1" cellpadding="0" style="float:right; background:transparent; margin:0; padding:0;">
<tr>
<td>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Grant_in_1885.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Grant sitting in a porch chair wrapped in blankets" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/US_Grant_in_1885.jpg/220px-US_Grant_in_1885.jpg" width="220" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/US_Grant_in_1885.jpg/330px-US_Grant_in_1885.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/US_Grant_in_1885.jpg/440px-US_Grant_in_1885.jpg 2x" data-file-width="884" data-file-height="1040" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Grant_in_1885.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant working on his memoirs in June 1885, less than a month before his death</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Drawing of steam engine and train approaching station with an honor guard at attention" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg/220px-Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg/330px-Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg/440px-Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2568" data-file-height="1881" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grant_Funeral_Train3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant's funeral train at West Point</div>
</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>To restore his family's income, Grant wrote several articles on his Civil War campaigns for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Century_Magazine" title="The Century Magazine">The Century Magazine</a></i> at $500 each. The articles were well received by critics, and the editor, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Underwood_Johnson" title="Robert Underwood Johnson">Robert Underwood Johnson</a>, suggested that Grant write a book of memoirs, as Sherman and others had done. Grant's articles would serve as the basis for several chapters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981494_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981494-311"><span>[</span>303<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the summer of 1884, Grant complained of a soreness in his throat, but put off seeing a doctor until late October where he finally learned it was <a href="/wiki/Head_and_neck_cancer" title="Head and neck cancer">throat cancer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaugh277_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaugh277-312"><span>[</span>304<span>]</span></a></sup> Before being diagnosed, Grant was invited to a Methodist service for Civil War veterans in <a href="/wiki/Ocean_Grove,_New_Jersey" title="Ocean Grove, New Jersey">Ocean Grove, New Jersey</a>, on August 4, 1884, receiving a standing ovation from more than ten thousand veterans and others; it would be his last public appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981495.E2.80.93496_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981495.E2.80.93496-313"><span>[</span>305<span>]</span></a></sup> In March of the following year, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times" class="mw-redirect">New York Times</a> finally announced that Grant was dying of cancer<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaugh279_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaugh279-314"><span>[</span>306<span>]</span></a></sup> and a nationwide public concern for the former president began.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a622.E2.80.93626_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a622.E2.80.93626-315"><span>[</span>307<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317"><span>[</span>i<span>]</span></a></sup> Later, Grant, who had forfeited his military pension when he assumed the presidency, was honored by his friends and the Congress when he was restored to the rank of General of the Army with full retirement pay.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith625_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith625-318"><span>[</span>309<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Despite his debilitating illness, Grant worked diligently on his memoirs at his home in New York City, and then from <a href="/wiki/Grant_Cottage_State_Historic_Site" title="Grant Cottage State Historic Site">a cottage</a> on the slopes of <a href="/wiki/Mount_McGregor_(mountain)" title="Mount McGregor (mountain)">Mount McGregor</a>, finishing only days before he died.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a629.E2.80.93630_319-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a629.E2.80.93630-319"><span>[</span>310<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant asked his former staff officer, <a href="/wiki/Adam_Badeau" title="Adam Badeau">Adam Badeau</a>, to help edit his work. Grant's son <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Dent_Grant" title="Frederick Dent Grant">Fred</a> assisted with references and proofreading. <i>Century</i> magazine offered Grant a book contract with a 10 percent royalty, but Grant accepted a better offer from his friend, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a>, who proposed a 75 percent royalty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981501.E2.80.93505_320-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981501.E2.80.93505-320"><span>[</span>311<span>]</span></a></sup> His memoir ends with the Civil War, and does not cover the post-war years, including his presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981511_321-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981511-321"><span>[</span>312<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The book, <i><a href="/wiki/Personal_Memoirs_of_Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Personal Memoirs of Ulysses S. Grant">Personal Memoirs of Ulysses S. Grant</a></i>, was a critical and commercial success. In the end, Julia Grant received about $450,000 in royalties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981501.E2.80.93505_320-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981501.E2.80.93505-320"><span>[</span>311<span>]</span></a></sup> The memoir has been highly regarded by the public, military historians, and literary critics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson131.E2.80.93173_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson131.E2.80.93173-322"><span>[</span>313<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant portrayed himself in the persona of the honorable Western hero, whose strength lies in his honesty and straightforwardness. He candidly depicted his battles against both the Confederates and internal army foes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell189.E2.80.93209_323-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell189.E2.80.93209-323"><span>[</span>314<span>]</span></a></sup> Twain called the <i>Memoirs</i> a "literary masterpiece." Given over a century of favorable literary analysis, reviewer <a href="/wiki/Mark_Perry_(author)" title="Mark Perry (author)">Mark Perry</a> states that the <i>Memoirs</i> are "the most significant work" of American <a href="/wiki/Non-fiction" title="Non-fiction">non-fiction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerry234.E2.80.93235_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerry234.E2.80.93235-324"><span>[</span>315<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the days preceding his death, Grant's wife, Julia, all of his children, and three grandchildren were present. After a year-long struggle with the cancer, Grant died at 8 o'clock in the morning in the Mount McGregor cottage on July 23, 1885, at the age of 63.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981517_325-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981517-325"><span>[</span>316<span>]</span></a></sup> Sheridan, then Commanding General of the Army, ordered a day-long tribute to Grant on all military posts, and President <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> ordered a thirty-day nationwide period of mourning. After private services, the honor guard placed Grant's body on a special <a href="/wiki/Funeral_train" title="Funeral train">funeral train</a>, which traveled to West Point and <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. A quarter of a million people viewed it in the two days before the funeral.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a633.E2.80.93635_326-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a633.E2.80.93635-326"><span>[</span>317<span>]</span></a></sup> Tens of thousands of men, many of them veterans from the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Army_of_the_Republic" title="Grand Army of the Republic">Grand Army of the Republic</a> (GAR) or other veterans' organizations, marched with Grant's casket drawn by two dozen horses to <a href="/wiki/Riverside_Park_(Manhattan)" title="Riverside Park (Manhattan)">Riverside Park</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Morningside_Heights,_Manhattan" title="Morningside Heights, Manhattan">Morningside Heights</a> neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Manhattan" title="Upper Manhattan">Upper Manhattan</a>, New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a633.E2.80.93635_326-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a633.E2.80.93635-326"><span>[</span>317<span>]</span></a></sup> His pallbearers included Union generals Sherman and Sheridan, Confederate generals Simon Bolivar Buckner and Joseph E. Johnston, Admiral David Dixon Porter, and Senator John A. Logan, the head of the GAR.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a633.E2.80.93635_326-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a633.E2.80.93635-326"><span>[</span>317<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grant's body was laid to rest in Riverside Park, first in a temporary tomb, and then—twelve years later, on April 17, 1897—in the <a href="/wiki/Grant%27s_Tomb" title="Grant's Tomb">General Grant National Memorial</a>, also known as "Grant's Tomb". The tomb is the largest <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum" title="Mausoleum">mausoleum</a> in North America. Attendance at the New York funeral topped 1.5 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a633.E2.80.93635_326-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a633.E2.80.93635-326"><span>[</span>317<span>]</span></a></sup> Ceremonies were held in other major cities around the country, and those who eulogized Grant in the press likened him to <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> and Abraham Lincoln.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaugh215.E2.80.93259_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaugh215.E2.80.93259-327"><span>[</span>318<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_reputation">Historical reputation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USA_grants_tomb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Neoclassical structure with dome" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/USA_grants_tomb.jpg/220px-USA_grants_tomb.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/USA_grants_tomb.jpg/330px-USA_grants_tomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/USA_grants_tomb.jpg/440px-USA_grants_tomb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1494" data-file-height="1992" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USA_grants_tomb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Grant%27s_Tomb" title="Grant's Tomb">General Grant National Memorial</a>, known colloquially as "Grant's Tomb", is the largest <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum" title="Mausoleum">mausoleum</a> in North America, and one of the largest in the world.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>No presidential reputations have changed as dramatically as Grant's.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner_2012_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner_2012-328"><span>[</span>319<span>]</span></a></sup> Hailed across the North as the winning general in a great war, his nomination as president seemed inevitable. Grant's popularity declined with congressional investigations into corruption in his administration and Custer's defeat at the Battle of the Little Big Horn. In 1877, there was bipartisan approval of Grant's peaceful handling of the electoral crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBadeau250_329-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBadeau250-329"><span>[</span>320<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant's reputation soared during his well-publicized world tour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012b45_330-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012b45-330"><span>[</span>321<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At his death, Grant was seen as "a symbol of the American national identity and memory", when millions turned out for his funeral procession in 1885 and attended the 1897 dedication of his tomb.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaugh2_331-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaugh2-331"><span>[</span>322<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant's popularity increased in the years immediately after his death. At the same time, commentators and scholars portrayed his administration as the most corrupt in American history. As the popularity of the pro-Confederate <a href="/wiki/Lost_Cause" title="Lost Cause" class="mw-redirect">Lost Cause</a> movement increased early in the 20th century, a more negative view became increasingly common.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981521-522_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981521-522-332"><span>[</span>323<span>]</span></a></sup> As they had early in the Civil War, Grant's new critics charged that he was a reckless drunk, and in light of his presidency, that he was also corrupt. In the 1930s, biographer <a href="/wiki/William_B._Hesseltine" title="William B. Hesseltine">William B. Hesseltine</a> noted that Grant's reputation deteriorated because his enemies were better writers than his friends.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith14_333-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith14-333"><span>[</span>324<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1931, Frederic Paxson and Christian Bach in the <i><a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_American_Biography" title="Dictionary of American Biography">Dictionary of American Biography</a></i> praised Grant's military vision and his execution of that vision in defeating the Confederacy, but of his political career, the authors were less complimentary. Speaking specifically of the scandals, they wrote that "personal scandal has not touched Grant in any plausible form, but it struck so close to him and so frequently as to necessitate the vindication of his honor by admitting his bad taste in the choice of associates."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaxson_.26_Bach500_334-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaxson_.26_Bach500-334"><span>[</span>325<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Views of Grant reached new lows as he was seen as an unsuccessful president and an unskilled, if lucky, general.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012b45_330-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012b45-330"><span>[</span>321<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Catton" title="Bruce Catton">Bruce Catton</a> and <a href="/wiki/T._Harry_Williams" title="T. Harry Williams">T. Harry Williams</a> began the reassessment of Grant's military career in the 1960s, shifting the analysis of Grant as victor by brute force to that of successful and skillful strategist and commander.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERafuse851_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERafuse851-335"><span>[</span>326<span>]</span></a></sup> Even for scholars with a particular concern for the plight of former slaves and Indians, Grant left a problematic legacy and, with changing attitudes toward warfare after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, Grant's military reputation suffered again.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERafuse855.2C_871.E2.80.93874_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERafuse855.2C_871.E2.80.93874-336"><span>[</span>327<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_S._McFeely" title="William S. McFeely">William S. McFeely</a> won the <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize_for_Biography_or_Autobiography" title="Pulitzer Prize for Biography or Autobiography">Pulitzer Prize</a> for his critical 1981 biography that emphasized the failure of Grant's presidency to carry out lasting progress and concluded that "he did not rise above limited talents or inspire others to do so in ways that make his administration a credit to American politics."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981522_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981522-337"><span>[</span>328<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Y._Simon" title="John Y. Simon">John Y. Simon</a> in 1982 responded to McFeely: "Grant's failure as President&#160;... lies in the failure of the Indian peace policy and the collapse of Reconstruction&#160;... But if Grant tried and failed, who could have succeeded?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon_1982221_338-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon_1982221-338"><span>[</span>329<span>]</span></a></sup> Simon praised Grant's first term in office, arguing that it should be "remembered for his staunch enforcement of the rights of freedmen combined with conciliation of former Confederates, for reform in Indian policy and civil service, for successful negotiation of the <i>Alabama</i> Claims, and for delivery of peace and prosperity."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon_2002253_339-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon_2002253-339"><span>[</span>330<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Simon, the Liberal Republican revolt, the Panic of 1873, and the North's conservative retreat from Reconstruction weakened Grant's second term in office, although his foreign policy remained steady.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimon_2002253_339-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimon_2002253-339"><span>[</span>330<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Historians' views have grown more favorable since the 1990s, appreciating Grant's protection of African Americans and his peace policy towards Indians, even where those policies failed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaugh2_331-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaugh2-331"><span>[</span>322<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant's reputation rose further with Jean Edward Smith's 2001 biography. Smith argued that the same qualities that made Grant a success as a general carried over to his political life to make him, if not a successful president, then certainly an admirable one.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeigley1105_340-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeigley1105-340"><span>[</span>331<span>]</span></a></sup> Smith wrote that "the common thread is strength of character—an indomitable will that never flagged in the face of adversity&#160;... Sometimes he blundered badly; often he oversimplified; yet he saw his goals clearly and moved toward them relentlessly."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith15_341-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith15-341"><span>[</span>332<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brooks_Simpson" title="Brooks Simpson" class="mw-redirect">Brooks Simpson</a> continued the trend in the first of two volumes on Grant in 2000, although the work was far from a hagiography.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERafuse862.E2.80.93863_342-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERafuse862.E2.80.93863-342"><span>[</span>333<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/H._W._Brands" title="H. W. Brands">H. W. Brands</a>, in his more uniformly positive 2012 book, wrote favorably of Grant's military and political careers alike, saying:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>As commanding general in the Civil War, he had defeated secession and destroyed slavery, secession's cause. As President during Reconstruction he had guided the South back into the Union. By the end of his public life the Union was more secure than at any previous time in the history of the nation. And no one had done more to produce the result than he.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a636_343-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a636-343"><span>[</span>334<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>As <a href="/wiki/Eric_Foner" title="Eric Foner">Eric Foner</a> wrote, Brands gave "a sympathetic account of Grant's forceful and temporarily successful effort as president to crush the Ku Klux Klan, which had inaugurated a reign of terror against the former slaves." Foner adds that by 1875 Grant had given up rescuing the blacks, saying the public was tired of them, concluding that "Grant's unwillingness to act reflected the broader Northern retreat from Reconstruction and its ideal of racial equality."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner_2012_328-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner_2012-328"><span>[</span>319<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Besides civil rights, issues of environmental protection have also attracted historiographical attention. Historian Joan Waugh, in her generally favorable book, says Grant appreciated the beauty of the West, and in 1872 signed the law establishing the country's first national park at <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone" title="Yellowstone" class="mw-redirect">Yellowstone</a>. However, she argues:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>...&#160;he approved of projects that took great swaths of land and opened them to timber, cattle, land speculators, and millions of settlers&#160;... the idea of preserving the region's environment and concerns about industrial pollution were of much less importance to Grant&#160;... <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaugh132_344-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaugh132-344"><span>[</span>335<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Throughout the 20th century, historians ranked his presidency near the bottom. In the 21st century, his military reputation is strong, while experts <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">rank his presidential achievements</a> well below average.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner_2012_328-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner_2012-328"><span>[</span>319<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials_and_presidential_library">Memorials and presidential library</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US-$50-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/US-%2450-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg/220px-US-%2450-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg" width="220" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/US-%2450-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg/330px-US-%2450-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/US-%2450-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg/440px-US-%2450-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1773" data-file-height="1550" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US-$50-GC-1928-Fr-2404.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grant has appeared on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_fifty-dollar_bill" title="United States fifty-dollar bill">United States fifty-dollar bill</a> since 1913.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_cultural_depictions" title="Ulysses S. Grant cultural depictions">Ulysses S. Grant cultural depictions</a></div>
<p>Several memorials honor Grant. In addition to his mausoleum, the General Grant National Memorial in New York, there are the <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_Memorial" title="Ulysses S. Grant Memorial">Ulysses S. Grant Memorial</a> at the foot of Capitol Hill on the mall in Washington, D.C.,<sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345"><span>[</span>336<span>]</span></a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant_National_Historic_Site" title="Ulysses S. Grant National Historic Site">Ulysses S. Grant National Historic Site</a> near St. Louis, and several other sites in Ohio and Illinois that honor his life.<sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346"><span>[</span>337<span>]</span></a></sup> There are smaller memorials in Chicago's <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Park" title="Lincoln Park">Lincoln Park</a> and Philadelphia's <a href="/wiki/Fairmount_Park" title="Fairmount Park">Fairmount Park</a>. Named in his honor are <a href="/wiki/Grant_Park_(Chicago)" title="Grant Park (Chicago)">Grant Park</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Grant_County,_Indiana" title="Grant County, Indiana">several counties in western and midwestern states</a>. From 1890 to 1940, part of what is now <a href="/wiki/Kings_Canyon_National_Park" title="Kings Canyon National Park">Kings Canyon National Park</a> was called <i>General Grant National Park</i>, named for the <a href="/wiki/General_Grant_tree" title="General Grant tree" class="mw-redirect">General Grant sequoia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347"><span>[</span>338<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant has appeared on the front of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_fifty-dollar_bill" title="United States fifty-dollar bill">United States fifty-dollar bill</a> since 1913, and <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidents_on_U.S._postage_stamps#Ulysses_S._Grant" title="U.S. presidents on U.S. postage stamps">appears on several stamps</a>.</p>
<p>In May 2012, the Ulysses S. Grant Foundation, on the institute's fiftieth anniversary, selected <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_State_University#Mitchell_Memorial_Library" title="Mississippi State University">Mississippi State University</a> as the permanent location for Ulysses S. Grant's <a href="/wiki/Presidential_library" title="Presidential library">presidential library</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348"><span>[</span>339<span>]</span></a></sup> Historian John Y. Simon edited Grant's letters into a 32-volume scholarly edition published by <a href="/wiki/Southern_Illinois_University_Press" title="Southern Illinois University Press">Southern Illinois University Press</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349"><span>[</span>340<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">One biographer, Edward G. Longacre, attributes Grant's parents' decision to their recognition of his hatred of music.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongacre6.E2.80.937_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongacre6.E2.80.937-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Grant, the S. did not stand for anything, though Hamer had used it to stand for Simpson, Grant's mother's maiden name.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198112Smith24.2C_83_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198112Smith24.2C_83-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to biographer McFeely, historians overwhelmingly agree that his drunkenness, although off duty, at the time was a fact, though there are no eyewitness reports extant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198155_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_198155-36"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-162"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-162">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Residents of Galena gave Grant the home in 1865 as thanks for his war service.<sup id="cite_ref-home_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-home-160"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> After his presidential term ended in 1877, Grant visited the home occasionally. Maintenance of the home as a memorial to Grant started in 1904 and continues today.<sup id="cite_ref-nhl_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhl-161"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-196"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-196">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1876, two decisions of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Reese" title="United States v. Reese">United States v. Reese</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Cruikshank" title="United States v. Cruikshank">United States v. Cruikshank</a></i>) also did much to undermine Reconstruction-era laws that protected the rights of black Americans. Grant nominee <a href="/wiki/Morrison_Waite" title="Morrison Waite">Morrison Waite</a> wrote the court's opinions joined by others, while another Grant nominee <a href="/wiki/Ward_Hunt" title="Ward Hunt">Ward Hunt</a> dissented.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981387.E2.80.93389_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981387.E2.80.93389-178"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-221"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-221">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The international tribunal awarded the United States $15,500,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith512.E2.80.93515_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith512.E2.80.93515-220"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-247"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-247">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Greeley died after election day but before the day the Electoral College voted, as a result, Greeley's running mate, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Gratz_Brown" title="Benjamin Gratz Brown">Benjamin Gratz Brown</a>, received most of the electoral votes Greeley would have had.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a499_246-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands_2012a499-246"><span>[</span>240<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-268"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-268">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">McFeely, writing in 1981, believed that Grant knew of Babcock's guilt, while Smith, in 2001, believed the evidence against Babcock was circumstantial at best.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981415Smith591_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFeely_1981415Smith591-267"><span>[</span>260<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-317"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-317">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Today, medical historians believed he suffered from a T<sub>1</sub>N<sub>1</sub> carcinoma of the tonsillar fossa.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenehan_.26_Lowry377.E2.80.93383_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenehan_.26_Lowry377.E2.80.93383-316"><span>[</span>308<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
